Two Types of Predication in Logic
Author distinguishes two types of predication and discusses their implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between predication of agreement (haml al-muwata'a) and predication of derivation (haml al-ishtiqaq) in Arabic logical propositions.

Core Idea
Not all predicates are equal: some are identical in essence, others derive from an attribute.

In logic, understanding how a predicate relates to its subject is fundamental. The author introduces two distinct types of predication: one where subject and predicate share the same essential reality, and another where the predicate is derived from an attribute of the subject. This distinction is crucial for analyzing propositions in Islamic sciences like Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of predication
Look at the relationship between the concept of the subject and the concept of the predicate in the Arabic expression.
2
Check conceptual identity
If the subject and predicate share the same essential reality (e.g., 'human is animal'), it is haml al-muwata'a.
The concepts are identical in essence, though terms differ.
3
Check derivational relationship
If the predicate is derived from an attribute of the subject (e.g., 'body is moving'), it is haml al-ishtiqaq.
The predicate term is derived from a source (e.g., 'moving' from 'motion').
4
Apply to examples
Given a proposition from Islamic sciences, determine which type it is and explain your reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الجهة من حيث الشخص المشار إليه فهو بالنسبة إلى كليه لا بد وأن يكون موضوعا. وقد علم أن حقيقة الموضوع والمحمول في حمل المواطاة هو أن يكون المفهوم من الموضوع هو المفهوم من المحمول بعينه، كقولنا: الإنسان حيوان، فالمفهوم من الإنسان هو المفهوم من الحيوان بعينه لا غير. وحمل الاشتقاق هو أن يكون الموصوف مشتقا من الصفة، كقولنا: الجسم متحرك، فإن مفهوم الجسم غير مفهوم المتحرك، بل الجسم موصوف بالحركة، فالمتحرك مشتق من الحركة. فالأول يسمى حمل المواطاة، والثاني يسمى حمل الاشتقاق.
As for the aspect of the indicated individual, it must be a subject in relation to its universal. It is known that the reality of subject and predicate in the predication of agreement (haml al-muwata'a) is that the concept from the subject is the very concept from the predicate, like our saying: 'The human is an animal,' for the concept from 'human' is the very concept from 'animal,' nothing else. And the predication of derivation (haml al-ishtiqaq) is that the described (al-mawsuf) is derived from the attribute, like our saying: 'The body is moving,' for the concept of 'body' is not the concept of 'moving'; rather, the body is described by motion, so 'moving' is derived from motion. The first is called predication of agreement, and the second is called predication of derivation.
Source pages p. 19

Commentary
This passage defines two fundamental types of predication in logic. The author begins by stating that the indicated individual, relative to its universal, must be a subject. Then he explains that in predication of agreement (haml al-muwata'a), the concept of the subject and the concept of the predicate are identical in essence. For example, 'The human is an animal' – here 'human' and 'animal' share the same essential reality, though they differ in specificity. In predication of derivation (haml al-ishtiqaq), the predicate is derived from an attribute that is not identical to the subject's essence. For example, 'The body is moving' – 'body' and 'moving' are not conceptually identical; rather, 'moving' is a derivative term describing the body's attribute. The first is named 'haml al-muwata'a' (predication of agreement) and the second 'haml al-ishtiqaq' (predication of derivation). This distinction is crucial for understanding how predicates relate to subjects in logical propositions, especially in the Islamic sciences where one must differentiate between essential predication (e.g., 'Zayd is human') and accidental predication (e.g., 'Zayd is standing').

Key Terms
حمل المواطاة
حمل الاشتقاق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) The wall is white.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between haml al-muwata'a and haml al-ishtiqaq is essential for correctly analyzing the logical form of propositions in Islamic sciences.

The Three Types of Propositions
Author classifies propositions into hamliyya, shartiyya muttasila, and shartiyya munfasila.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given proposition as hamliyya, shartiyya muttasila, or shartiyya munfasila based on the type of judgment and connection.

Core Idea
Propositions divide into categorical, conditional conjunctive, and conditional disjunctive based on unconditional judgment, entailment, or opposition.

In this passage, Razi presents the foundational classification of propositions (qadaya) into three types. Understanding this classification is crucial for analyzing arguments in Islamic logic, as different types of propositions form the building blocks of syllogisms. The examples given—'Man is a writer', 'Whenever the sun rises, daytime exists', and 'Either the number is even or odd'—illustrate each type clearly.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basis of division
Note that the division is based on whether the judgment (hukm) in the proposition is unconditional or conditioned upon something. Hamliyya has unconditional judgment; the others are conditioned.
2
Recognize hamliyya
A hamliyya (categorical) proposition has a judgment that is asserted with certainty without any condition. Example: 'Man is an animal' affirms the predicate unconditionally.
3
Recognize shartiyya muttasila
A shartiyya muttasila (conditional conjunctive) proposition involves a connection of entailment (luzum) between two parts. Example: 'Whenever the sun rises, daytime exists' – the existence of daytime follows from sunrise.
4
Recognize shartiyya munfasila
A shartiyya munfasila (conditional disjunctive) proposition involves a connection of opposition (inad) between two alternatives. Example: 'Either the number is even or odd' – the two disjuncts are mutually exclusive and exhaustive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقسيم القضايا: وهي قد تكون حملية كقولنا: الإنسان كاتب، وشرطية متصلة كقولنا: كلما كانت المطالعة فالنهار موجود، وشرطية منفصلة كقولنا: أما أن يكون العدد واجبا أو فردا. والحكم في القضية إما أن يكون موقوفا على شرط أو لا يكون. والثاني هو الحملي لأن الحكم في قولنا الإنسان حيوان حاصل جزمًا. وأما الأول فإما أن تكون تعلقه بذلك الشرط تعلق اللزوم سواء كان الملازمة ذاتية ولكن بالاتفاق وهو المتصل، أو تعلق العناد وهو المنفصل. واعلم أن الحملي والمتصل والفصل هو الموجبة أما السالبة فلا، لأن إذا قلنا زيد ليس بقائم فقد دفعنا الحمل ومع رفع الحمل كيف يتحقق الحمل وكذا المتصل والمنفصل إلا أن جواز السوالب سكانها استعدادا لقبول الحمل أو الاتصال أو الانفصال فلهذا سميت حملية ومتصلة ومنفصلة لأجل ذلك الاستعداد على سبيل المجاز. واعلم أن تسمية المتصلة بالشرطية بسيطة للعربية، وأما تسمية المنفصلة بها مجاز، لأنهم لما سموا المتصلة شرطية وكان الحكم منها غير جازم سموا كل ما كان الحكم فيها غير جازم شرطية. والكلام في أركانها وأحكامها. أما الأركان فهي أنا صورتها.
Division of propositions: They may be categorical (hamliyya) like our saying 'Man is a writer', and conditional conjunctive (shartiyya muttasila) like our saying 'Whenever the sun rises, daytime exists', and conditional disjunctive (shartiyya munfasila) like our saying 'Either the number is even or odd'. And the judgment in the proposition is either conditioned upon a condition or not. The second is the categorical because the judgment in our saying 'Man is an animal' is asserted with certainty. As for the first, either its connection to that condition is a connection of entailment (luzum), whether it is essential but by agreement, and it is the conjunctive, or a connection of opposition (inad), and it is the disjunctive. Know that the categorical, the conjunctive, and the disjunctive are affirmative; as for the negative, it is not, because if we say 'Zayd is not standing', we have removed the attribution, and with removal of attribution how is attribution realized? And similarly for the conjunctive and disjunctive, except that the possibility of negatives in them is due to their readiness to accept attribution, connection, or disconnection. Therefore they are called categorical, conjunctive, and disjunctive for that readiness metaphorically. Know that naming the conjunctive as 'shartiyya' is straightforward in Arabic; as for naming the disjunctive as such, it is metaphorical, because when they named the conjunctive 'shartiyya' and its judgment was non-categorical, they called everything whose judgment is non-categorical 'shartiyya'. The discussion is on their pillars and rulings. As for the pillars, they are...
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage provides a classification of propositions (qadaya) into three types: hamliyya (categorical), shartiyya muttasila (conditional conjunctive), and shartiyya munfasila (conditional disjunctive). Examples are given: 'Man is a writer' (categorical), 'Whenever the sun rises, daytime exists' (conjunctive), and 'Either the number is even or odd' (disjunctive). The judgment (hukm) in a proposition is either unconditional (as in categorical, e.g., 'Man is an animal' is asserted with certainty) or conditioned upon a condition. The conditioned type further divides into connection of entailment (luzum) for conjunctive and connection of opposition (inad) for disjunctive. The author notes that only affirmative propositions are properly categorized as such; negative propositions (e.g., 'Zayd is not standing') are not instances of attribution or connection in actuality, but are named metaphorically based on their potentiality (isti'dad). Additionally, the term 'shartiyya' (conditional) is applied straightforwardly to conjunctive conditionals because their judgment is non-categorical (ghayr jazim), while disjunctive conditionals are called 'shartiyya' only metaphorically, by extension, since their judgment is also non-categorical. The passage ends beginning the discussion of the pillars (arkan) of propositions.

Key Terms
حملي
شرطي متصل
شرطي منفصل
حكم جازم
استعداد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Razi, which type of proposition involves a judgment that is unconditional?
A) Hamliyya
B) Shartiyya muttasila
C) Shartiyya munfasila
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
Propositions are classified into categorical (hamliyya) and two conditionals (muttasila and munfasila) based on whether judgment is unconditional or conditioned by entailment or opposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

Judgment in Propositions: Unconditional vs Conditioned and Metaphorical Naming of Negatives
Author discusses unconditional vs conditioned judgment and metaphorical naming of negatives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why negative propositions are named after positive types metaphorically based on isti'dad (potentiality).

Core Idea
Negative propositions are not truly categorical or conditional, but are named by metaphor due to their potential to accept attribution or connection.

Razi delves deeper into the nature of judgment (hukm) in propositions. He explains that only affirmative propositions are genuine instances of the three types, while negatives are named by metaphor based on their readiness (isti'dad). This nuance is important for precise logical analysis, especially when dealing with negations in Islamic theological and legal arguments.

Guided Path
1
Understand the difference between affirmative and negative
Identify that in a negative proposition like 'Zayd is not standing', the predicate is removed (refʿ al-ḥaml), so it does not truly have the structure of a categorical proposition.
2
Grasp the concept of isti'dad
Isti'dad means readiness or potentiality. Although the negative proposition lacks actual attribution or connection, it has the potential to be transformed into an affirmative or to carry a conditional structure. This potentiality justifies the metaphorical naming.
3
Apply the metaphorical naming principle
Recognize that the term 'shartiyya' is applied straightforwardly to conjunctive conditionals because their judgment is non-categorical (ghayr jazim). Disjunctive conditionals are called 'shartiyya' only metaphorically, by extension, because they also share the property of non-categorical judgment.

Propositions

5 min
The Three Types of Propositions in Islamic Logic
Author classifies propositions into three types with examples and clarifies the role of affirmation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify any proposition from an Islamic text as categorical, conjunctive conditional, or disjunctive conditional, and explain why negative propositions are only metaphorically given those names.

Core Idea
Propositions divide into unconditional (categorical) and conditional (if-then or either-or).

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi explains the foundational classification of propositions in Islamic logic. Understanding these three types—categorical, conditional conjunctive, and conditional disjunctive—is essential for analyzing arguments in kalam, fiqh, and usul. We will walk through his examples and the key distinction: whether the judgment depends on a condition. You will also learn why negative propositions are only metaphorically called categorical, etc.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types listed by the author
In the passage, al-Razi enumerates three types: categorical (hamliyya), conditional conjunctive (shartiyya muttasila), and conditional disjunctive (shartiyya munfasila). Note the Arabic terms and their English equivalents.
2
Recognize the defining feature: presence or absence of a condition
The author states that the judgment in a proposition is either dependent on a condition or not. Categorical propositions have no condition; conditional propositions have a condition that is either implicative (luzum) or opposition (inad).
Implication can be by agreement (ittifaq) or necessity (darura).
3
Examine the examples given for each type
The examples: categorical: 'Man is a writer' (judgment unconditional); conjunctive: 'Whenever there is reading, then day exists' (if-then); disjunctive: 'Either the number is even or it is odd' (exclusive alternatives).
These examples from the source show the typical structure.
4
Understand why negative propositions are not truly categorical
Al-Razi explains that the terms hamliyya, muttasila, munfasila apply properly to affirmative propositions. A negative like 'Man is not a writer' negates the predication itself, so it is only metaphorically called categorical due to the potential for affirmation.
This nuance prevents misclassification of negative propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تقسيم القضية: وهي قد تكون حملية كقولنا الإنسان كاتب، وشرطية متصلة كقولنا كلما كانت المطالعة فالنهار موجود، وشرطية منفصلة كقولنا أما أن يكون العدد متحدا وأما أن يكون فردا. والحكم في القضية إما أن يكون موقوفا على شرط أو لا يكون. والثاني هو الحملية لأن الحكم في قولنا الإنسان حيوان حاصل جزما ومعنى غير موقوف على شرط. وأما الأول فإما أن تكون تعلقه بذلك الشرط تعلق اللزوم سواء كان الاتفاق أو الضرورة وهو المتصل، أو تعلق العناد وهو المنفصل. واعلم أن الحملية والمتصلة والمنفصلة هي الموجبة، أما السالبة فلا، لأنه إذا قلنا ليس الإنسان كاتبا فقد دفعنا الحمل. ومع رفع الحمل كيف يتحقق الحمل؟ وكذا المتصل والمنفصل إلا أن جواز السوالب يكونها استعدادا لقبول الحمل أو الاتصال أو الانفصال، فلذلك سميت حملية ومتصلة ومنفصلة، وذلك الاستعداد على سبيل المجاز.
Division of the proposition: It is either categorical (hamliyya) like our saying 'Man is a writer', or conditional conjunctive (shartiyya muttasila) like our saying 'Whenever there is reading, then day exists', or conditional disjunctive (shartiyya munfasila) like our saying 'Either the number is even or it is odd'. And the judgment in the proposition is either dependent on a condition or not. The second is the categorical because the judgment in our saying 'Man is an animal' is obtained definitively and its meaning is not dependent on a condition. As for the first, it is either its connection to that condition is a connection of implication, whether by agreement or necessity, which is the conjunctive; or a connection of opposition, which is the disjunctive. And know that the categorical, conjunctive, and disjunctive are the affirmative ones. As for the negative, it is not, because if we say 'Man is not a writer', we have negated the predication. And with the negation of predication, how is predication realized? Similarly for the conjunctive and disjunctive, except that the permissibility of negatives is that they have a potentiality to accept predication, connection, or disjunction, so they are called categorical, conjunctive, and disjunctive, and that potentiality is metaphorical.
Source pages p. 50

Commentary
This passage defines the three types of propositions in logic. The author first enumerates the divisions: categorical (hamliyya) which asserts a predicate of a subject without condition, conditional conjunctive (shartiyya muttasila) which asserts a conditional implication, and conditional disjunctive (shartiyya munfasila) which asserts an exclusive disjunction. Examples are given from common Arabic statements: 'Man is a writer' (categorical), 'Whenever there is reading, then day exists' (conjunctive conditional), and 'Either the number is even or it is odd' (disjunctive conditional). Then he explains that what distinguishes them is whether the judgment is dependent on a condition or not. Categorical propositions have unconditional judgments; they affirm a predicate of a subject directly. Conditionals have judgments dependent on a condition: in the conjunctive, the relationship is one of implication (luzum) whether by agreement (ittifaq) or necessity (darura); in the disjunctive, it is one of opposition (inad). He clarifies that these terms—hamliyya, muttasila, munfasila—properly apply to affirmative propositions. Negative propositions are only metaphorically given these names because they negate the very relation (predication, connection, or disjunction) that defines the type. For example, 'Man is not a writer' denies the predication itself, so calling it 'categorical' is merely metaphorical, indicating a potential for affirmation. This careful distinction is crucial for understanding how logicians classify and discuss propositions.

Key Terms
قضية
حملية
شرطية متصلة
شرطية منفصلة
لزوم
عناد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a conditional conjunctive proposition (shartiyya muttasila)?
A) 'Zakat is obligatory'
B) 'If you travel, then shorten the prayer'
C) 'Either the marriage is valid or null'
D) 'Man is not a writer'

Key Takeaway
Propositions are divided into three types based on conditionality, and the names apply strictly to affirmatives; negatives are metaphorical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Matter and Form of Propositions
Author defines matter (subject and predicate) and form (relation) of propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the matter and form of a proposition and identify the subject, predicate, and relation in any categorical proposition.

Core Idea
A proposition's matter is its subject and predicate; its form is the relation between them.

In this passage, the author breaks down a proposition into its fundamental components. Understanding the distinction between matter (subject and predicate) and form (the relation) is essential for analyzing logical statements in Islamic sciences like Kalam and Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and predicate
In any categorical proposition, locate the subject (mawdu') – what is being talked about – and the predicate (mahmul) – what is being said about the subject. These are the matter (al-madda).
Example: In 'The world is temporally originated', 'the world' is the subject, 'temporally originated' is the predicate.
2
Recognize the relation (nisba)
The relation between subject and predicate is the form (al-sura). The author explains that this relation can be considered either as a meaning (ma'na) or as a word (lafz).
The relation is the judgment that connects subject and predicate.
3
Distinguish meaning from word
The form as meaning is the actual mental connection; the form as word is the linguistic expression of that connection (the copula). The author will discuss the copula later.
This distinction is crucial for understanding how logical structure maps onto language.
4
Apply to a sample proposition
Take the proposition 'Every intoxicant is forbidden.' Identify the matter (subject: 'every intoxicant', predicate: 'forbidden') and form (the relation of 'is'). Is the relation explicit or implicit?

وهى النسبة من طرفها واما مادتها وهي المحموك الموضوعية اما الصوره فالبحث اما ان نقع عن معناها واما عن اللفظ الدال عليها اما المعنى فصه محتان ناكل قضية مفها لا محاله دا الموضوع ودار المحمولك النسبه الى بينهما وفى مقاره لها إنفجار تفعل كل واحد منها مع الدخول عنها وتعقلها مع الدخول عن خصوصيه كل واحد منها ولان النسبه من البشر ساحه عنهما و المتاخر مغارب نسبه احد مما الى الاورغى نيه الاخر اليدان نسسه اخدمها إلى الفرنسيه الموصوفيه والمحلية ونسبه الأخر إلى الأول نسيه الحالية والوصفية ومد يكون أحدها بالوجوب والا خربی با امكان الدلك لم حفظ القضايا الجهات عند العلوم لكن المفنسه التذهي فرو واهيه العفيه هي موصوفية دات الموضوع بالمحمول واما الاخرى فخا ر جد لازمة واما اللفظ يفته خمسه ابحاث في ان كان النسه مدلولا عليها تهمنا في الرسم المحمول كما في المشعات والكلمات لم يجزا فرادها بالمطابقة والا وقع التكرار و هك القصه ثائية في اللفظ بالطبع ب المكان الطبيعي للرابطة المتوسط بين الموضوع والمحمول لان المنسه واقعه بيهما فاللفظ الدال لابد وان موسطها عليها ج کل فضہ فھی نے تمہا رباعيه لانه لا بد للرابطة 2 لفها من كيفية مخصوصة اما الضرورة او الاضرورة اما اللفظ فق الكون وقد يكون فاذا بلنا الانسان واجب ان تكون حتوانا احتمل ان لون الواجب محمولا وذكره ما بعد لیکو در سالهالات الوجوب امرنيتي فلا ما ذكره مخصوصه الأبناك المني الله وأتكون جر. وكل منه وان يكون خارجا عند فعل المقدير الاول والثاني لا القضية موجعة في اللفظ بل مطلقة بل إنما تكون موجعة على التقدير الثالث ی شکكاب فا كل عمود فان نسبته الى موضوعه اما بالوجوب او الامكان أو لا تباع فانصح جعد هذه السنة محمولاً لوجه لمنه كان بوتها لموضوعنا على احدى هذه الجمال فلام التلات لكن سلمنا امكان جعلها تحولات وجد المنها وخارجا عنها فيم نمير بعض هذه الاحتمالات عن بعض والجواب عز الا وان ذلك عايلهم الوجعلنا هه الثلثة أمود النونية في الخارج لكن ليس الأمركز كل ما سيحمر في الحكمة وعن الثانيات الرابطة ان تقدمتها كانت محمولات أوجد امنتها و ان اخوت كانت جنات وانظم لکن مذكورة فبالنية السور على ماسيا تى تفسيره ان شاء الله تعالى
And it is the relation from its two sides. As for its matter, it is the predicate and the subject. As for the form, the investigation is either about its meaning or about the word indicating it. As for the meaning, in it [there are] two considerations: [the] proposition necessarily has the subject itself and the predicate itself and the relation between them. And in its comparison, [each one] occurs with the consideration of the other and its conception with the consideration of the particularity of each. And because the relation is [a matter] of scope, it is subsequent to both. [There is] a relation of one to the other: [the relation] of the first to the second is the relation of being described and [the relation] of the second to the first is the relation of description. And one may be by necessity and the other by possibility. That is why propositions with modalities are preserved in the sciences, but the [real] conception is the description of the subject by the predicate. And the other is external, not necessary. As for the word, it requires five investigations: whether the relation is indicated in the form of the predicate as in [certain expressions] and words, it is not permissible to combine them by matching otherwise repetition occurs. And thus the narrative continues in the word by nature in the natural place of the copula between the subject and the predicate because the relation occurs between them, so the indicating word must be intermediate upon them. Every such proposition is quadruple because the copula necessarily has a specific quality: either necessity or possibility or non-necessity. As for the word, it is 'being' and may be. So when we say 'Man is necessarily an animal', it is possible that the word 'necessary' is a predicate and its mention [after] ... and it is possible that it is external in the first and second estimation, the proposition is not modal in expression but absolute; it is only modal in the third estimation. ... every pillar, its relation to its subject is either by necessity or possibility or negation. ... we accept the possibility of making it modes, and we find them external and internal, so how do we distinguish some of these possibilities from others? The answer is that [it is] upon them, and we made these three modes as extraneous in reality, but it is not so, everything that is determined in wisdom ... and regarding the second [point] the copula, if it preceded, then [it would be] predicates; if it came later, then it is [part of] the subject ... but it is mentioned, so by the intention of the soura as will be explained if God wills.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage discusses the matter and form of propositions (qadaya). The author explains that a proposition has two material parts: the subject (mawdu') and the predicate (mahmul), and a formal part: the relation (nisba) between them. The relation can be understood either as the meaning (the actual connection) or the word (the copula). The author notes that the relation can be of two types: the predicate qualifying the subject (موصوفية) and the subject qualifying the predicate (حالية/وصفية), and each may be necessary or possible. This leads to the classification of propositions by modality (jihaat). The author then turns to the linguistic expression, stating that the word indicating the proposition requires five investigations. He asserts that the natural place for the copula is between the subject and predicate, as it signifies the relation. This makes the proposition tripartite (subject, copula, predicate). However, sometimes the copula is not explicit, leading to a bipartite structure. The author then discusses a potential confusion: when a modal word like 'necessarily' is used, it might be mistaken for a predicate or an external modifier. He considers three possibilities: the modality is part of the predicate, external to the proposition, or a separate element. The resolution is that modalities are external to the real judgment but are indicated in the sentence.

Key Terms
المادة (al-madda): The matter of a proposition refers to the subject and the predicate.
الصورة (al-sura): The form of a proposition is the relation (nisba) between subject and predicate, and can be considered either as meaning or as verbal expression.
الرابطة (al-rabita): The copula is the word that signifies the relation between subject and predicate, typically 'is' or its equivalents.
الجهة (al-jiha): The modality of a proposition, such as necessity, possibility, or impossibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what are the two components of the matter of a proposition?
A) The subject and the predicate.
B) The copula and the relation.
C) The modality and the word.
D) The form and the meaning.

Key Takeaway
Every categorical proposition consists of matter (subject and predicate) and form (the relation between them).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the proposition 'Every intoxicant is forbidden.' Identify the subject, predicate, and the relation. Then explain whether the relation is explicit or implicit.

Propositions
beginner
5 min
The Copula and Tripartite vs Bipartite Division
Author discusses the copula and its role in tripartite/bipartite division.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the copula in a proposition and explain why a proposition can be tripartite or bipartite.

Core Idea
The copula signifies the relation; its presence makes a proposition tripartite; its absence makes it bipartite.

The author discusses the linguistic expression of the proposition. The copula (rabita) is the word indicating the relation, typically 'is' in Arabic. Its position is naturally between subject and predicate.

Guided Path
1
Locate the copula
In an Arabic proposition like 'al-insanu hayawanun' (Man is an animal), identify if there is an explicit copula. Sometimes it is implicit (bipartite), sometimes explicit (tripartite).
The copula is often omitted in Arabic nominal sentences.
2
Distinguish tripartite vs bipartite
When the copula is explicit, the proposition has three parts: subject, copula, predicate. When implicit, it has two parts: subject and predicate, with the relation understood.
The author asserts that the natural place for the copula is between subject and predicate.
3
Test the natural position
The author argues that because the relation occurs between subject and predicate, the word indicating the relation (copula) must be placed between them. Verify this with examples.
For example, 'Zayd is standing' should be structured as 'Zayd + is + standing'.
4
Practice with Fiqh examples
Take the proposition 'Al-salatu wajibatun' (Prayer is obligatory). Is the copula explicit? If not, rewrite it as a tripartite proposition by adding 'huwa' or 'kana' appropriately.

Propositions
beginner
5 min
Modality in Propositions
Author explains modality and resolves confusion about its place.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the modality (jiha) in a proposition and distinguish between modal and absolute propositions.

Core Idea
Modality qualifies the relation; it is external to the subject and predicate but indicated in the sentence.

The author explains that beyond the subject and predicate, propositions can carry a modality (necessity, possibility, impossibility). He resolves a confusion about whether the modal word is part of the predicate or a separate element.

Guided Path
1
Identify the modal word
Look for words like 'necessarily' (wajib), 'possibly' (mumkin), or 'impossibly' (mumtani') in the proposition. These indicate the modality of the relation.
Example: In 'Man is necessarily an animal', 'necessarily' is the modal word.
2
Test the three estimations
The author considers three ways to interpret the modal word: (1) as part of the predicate, (2) as external to the proposition, (3) as a separate mode indicating the quality of the relation. The author argues that only the third makes the proposition truly modal.
The first two estimations yield an absolute proposition; the third yields a modal proposition.
3
Distinguish modal from absolute
A modal proposition explicitly qualifies the relation with necessity, possibility, or impossibility. An absolute proposition does not; it simply asserts the relation without qualification.
For example, 'The sky is blue' is absolute; 'The sky is necessarily blue' is modal (if necessity is intended).
4
Apply to a Kalam example
Consider 'God is necessarily existent.' Identify the modality. Is it internal or external? According to the passage, the modality (necessity) is external to the real judgment but indicated in the sentence.

The author explains that a proposition has two material parts: the subject (mawdu') and the predicate (mahmul), and a formal part: the relation (nisba) between them. The relation can be understood either as the meaning (the actual connection) or the word (the copula). The author notes that the relation can be of two types: the predicate qualifying the subject (موصوفية) and the subject qualifying the predicate (حالية/وصفية), and each may be necessary or possible. This leads to the classification of propositions by modality (jihaat). The author then turns to the linguistic expression, stating that the word indicating the proposition requires five investigations. He asserts that the natural place for the copula is between the subject and predicate, as it signifies the relation. This makes the proposition tripartite (subject, copula, predicate). However, sometimes the copula is not explicit, leading to a bipartite structure. The author then discusses a potential confusion: when a modal word like 'necessarily' is used, it might be mistaken for a predicate or an external modifier. He considers three possibilities: the modality is part of the predicate, external to the proposition, or a separate element. The resolution is that modalities are external to the real judgment but are indicated in the sentence.

Key Terms
المادة (al-madda): The matter of a proposition refers to the subject and the predicate.
الصورة (al-sura): The form of a proposition is the relation (nisba) between subject and predicate, and can be considered either as meaning or as verbal expression.
الرابطة (al-rabita): The copula is the word that signifies the relation between subject and predicate, typically 'is' or its equivalents.
الجهة (al-jiha): The modality of a proposition, such as necessity, possibility, or impossibility.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why might a proposition with a modal word be considered absolute rather than modal?
A) Because the modal word is considered part of the predicate.
B) Because the modality is external in the first two estimations.
C) Because the proposition lacks a subject.
D) Because the copula is implicit.

Key Takeaway
Modality is external to the proposition's matter; it qualifies the relation, not the subject or predicate directly.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider 'The Prophet ﷺ is necessarily truthful.' Identify the modality and explain whether it is internal or external to the proposition.

Absolute Negation Is Inconceivable
Author argues absolute negation is inconceivable

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why absolute negation is inconceivable and identify the positive content presupposed by any negative proposition.

Core Idea
Every negative judgment secretly leans on an affirmation.

You might think that a negative statement like 'A is not B' is purely negative. But the author of this passage challenges that intuition. He argues that for any proposition to be meaningful, the mind must grasp something positive. In this lesson, we will unpack his reasoning and see why absolute negation—a negation with no positive basis—is logically impossible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key claim
The author states that 'absolute negation is inconceivable' (السلب المطلق غير معقول). Locate this claim in the Arabic.
The text is corrupted, but the phrase is discernible.
2
Understand the reason: every conception requires a positive essence
The author argues that for anything to be conceived (ma'qul), it must have an essence (nafsiyya) that the mind can point to. Without a positive essence, the mind cannot form a matching indication.
This is the core of the argument.
3
Apply to negative propositions
Consider a negative proposition like 'Zayd is not writing'. The concept 'Zayd' and the concept 'writing' are both positive. The negation only denies the relation. So the negative proposition depends on these positive concepts.
Absolute negation would deny everything, leaving no positive content to grasp.
4
Test with Islamic examples
Take the statement 'The world is not eternal' from kalam. This negation presupposes the positive concepts 'world' and 'eternal'. Without them, the negation would be meaningless.
This shows the principle operates in real Islamic sciences.

The author also notes that each affirmative proposition has a corresponding negation, but the negation itself depends on the affirmation for its intelligibility.

What Is Negation? The Problem of Absolute Negation
Defines negation and addresses absolute negation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define negation (salb) and analyze why absolute negation is considered unintelligible in Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Negation is judgment without affirmation; absolute negation is inconceivable because every known thing has an essence.

In logic, negation is more than just saying 'no'—it is a specific kind of judgment. The classical logicians asked: can we conceive of pure negation, with no positive content? Fakhr al-Din al-Razi addresses this in his Mulakhkhas. In this lesson, we unpack his definition of negation and the well-known objection that absolute negation is unintelligible.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Definition of Salb
Find where al-Razi defines negation. He says: 'al-salb huwa al-hukm bi-la thubut' (negation is judgment without affirmation). Note that it is a judgment, not just a word.
This definition emphasizes that negation is still a judgment—it relates subject and predicate, but denies the predicate.
2
Understand the Objection about Absolute Negation
The passage states that absolute negative (al-salib al-mutlaq) is unintelligible (ghayr ma'qul). The reason given: every intelligible has an essence (nafsaniyya); otherwise the mind cannot point to it. Pure negation would lack something to point to.
This objection likely comes from earlier debates about whether negation can be a concept on its own.
3
Examine the Response
Al-Razi gives the 'well-known view' (al-mashhur): there is no extension (bast) in negation. This means negation does not add a positive meaning; it merely denies. A negative proposition still needs an affirmative term in its convertible ('aks).
The response clarifies that negation is dependent on affirmation—you cannot have a purely negative concept without reference to an affirmative.
4
Relate to the Requirement of Essence
Consider why the mind cannot grasp a pure negation. For example, if I say 'not something', you have no definite idea. But if I say 'not a mountain', you first conceive 'mountain' then negate it. Absolute negation would be like 'not' without any content, which is impossible.
This underscores the principle that knowledge requires a positive basis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والسلب هو الحكم بلا ثبوت. لكن السلبي المطلق غير معقول لأن كل معقول حتمي أن له نفسية عن عين وإلا لم يتمكن العقل من الإشارة إليه دون أن يشير إلى مطابقة. والجواب: المشهور أنه لا بسط في السلب لا على معنى أن الإيجاب خلاف السلب بل على معنى أن السلب لا يعني أن يكون مذكورا والمعلوم الأبعد أن يكون كذلك لأن السلب المطلق غير معقول ابتداء والقضية السالبة تحتاج إلى الموجب في المتقولة. ثم الاعتبار في كون القضية موجبة أو سالبة بإثارة العلم أو نفيه لا يكون المحكوم عليه والمحكوم به ثبوتيا أو عدميا. مثاله: زيد غير عالم فقد حكمت على زيد أنه لا عالم فالقضية موجبة. والدليل عليه أن في الشرطية التي فيها اللزوم كانت موجبة سواء كان الطرفان ثبوتيين أو عدميين أو مختلطين، كقولك: كلما لم يكن الإنسان جبلا لم يكن عالمة فقد أثبت اللزوم بين عدم الجبلية وعدم العالمية فيكون الشرطية موجبة لأن اللزوم ثابت وإن كان كل واحد من طرفيها عدما. فالحاصل أنه متى كان المحمول أو منها كانت القضية معدولة لأنك ربطت إحدى الحكم بلا؟ وإن لم يكن هرا من ماهية واحد مني كان لا محالة خارجا عنها رافعا للحرف النسبية التي لها. فقد ظهر الفرق بين المعدولة والسالبة في نفس الأمر. وأما أنه كيف يغير اللفظ؟
Negation is judgment without affirmation. But absolute negative is unintelligible because every intelligible necessarily has an essence in itself; otherwise the mind cannot point to it without pointing to a correspondence. The answer: the well-known view is that there is no extension in negation, not in the sense that affirmation is contrary to negation, but in the sense that negation does not mean that it is mentioned, and the remote known is such [؟] because absolute negation is unintelligible initially, and the negative proposition needs the affirmative in the convertible. Then the consideration regarding whether the proposition is affirmative or negative is by [؟] knowledge or its negation; the subject and predicate are not [؟] affirmative or non-existent. Example: Zayd is not knowledgeable; you have ruled that Zayd is not knowledgeable, so the proposition is affirmative. The evidence for this is that in the conditional that contains implication, it is affirmative whether the two terms are affirmative or negative or mixed, as you say: Whenever man is not a mountain, he is not knowledgeable; you have affirmed the implication between the absence of mountainness and the absence of knowledge, so the conditional is affirmative because the implication is established even though each of its two terms is a non-existence. The conclusion is that whenever the predicate or [؟] is from it, the proposition is modified (ma'dula) because you have connected one of the judgments with [؟]? And if it is not from the essence of one, it is necessarily extrinsic to it, removing the relational letter that belongs to it. Thus the difference between the modified and the negative has appeared in reality. As for how the wording changes [؟].
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage defines negation (salb) as 'judgment without affirmation' and then addresses a difficulty: absolute negative propositions (al-salib al-mutlaq) are claimed to be unintelligible because every intelligible must have an essence that can be pointed to. The author then gives the well-known response that negation is not 'extended' meaning it does not signify a positive concept; rather, a negative proposition requires an affirmative term in its convertible. Next, the author clarifies the distinction between negative propositions (saliba) and modified propositions (ma'dula) by considering the nature of the judgment. In a modified proposition like 'Zayd is not-knowledgeable' (Zayd ghairu 'alim), the judgment is actually affirmative because it posits non-knowledge of Zayd; the proposition affirms a negative predicate. In contrast, a true negative proposition 'Zayd is not knowledgeable' (Zayd laysa bi-'alim) negates the predicate. The proof is given using a conditional example: 'Whenever man is not a mountain, he is not knowledgeable' has two negative terms but the implication (luzum) between them is affirmative; thus the conditional is affirmative in meaning. Therefore, whenever the predicate is a negative term, the proposition is modified (ma'dula) because the judgment links a non-essential attribute. The key difference is that saliba negates the predicate from the subject, while ma'dula affirms a negative predicate. This distinction is crucial for understanding proposition types in logic, especially in syllogistic where converting negative propositions requires care.

السلبي المطلق
الحكم

D) Because the well-known view has no extension.

Key Takeaway
Negation is judgment without affirmation, and pure negation is inconceivable because every concept must have a positive essence that the mind can grasp.

Modified vs. Negative Propositions: The Key Distinction
Distinguishes modified and negative propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a modified proposition (ma'dula) and a negative proposition (saliba) and correctly classify examples from Islamic logical and legal reasoning.

Core Idea
A modified proposition affirms a negative predicate; a negative proposition denies a positive predicate.

In Arabic logic, the phrasing of a proposition can be tricky. 'Zayd is not knowledgeable' is a negative proposition (saliba), but 'Zayd is non-knowledgeable' is a modified proposition (ma'dula) because it affirms 'non-knowledge' of Zayd. This distinction, clarified by al-Razi, is essential for correctly analyzing arguments, especially in conditional propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Judgment Type
Look at the proposition and decide whether it affirms or denies a predicate. In 'Zayd is non-knowledgeable' (Zayd ghairu 'alim), the judgment affirms the predicate 'non-knowledgeable'—so it is affirmative. In 'Zayd is not knowledgeable' (Zayd laysa bi-'alim), the judgment denies 'knowledgeable'—so it is negative.
The key is the copula: 'laysa' indicates negation; 'ghairu' indicates a modified predicate.
2
Compare the Wording
A modified proposition uses a negative term for the predicate (e.g., 'ghairu', 'la' as part of the predicate). A negative proposition uses a negative particle (e.g., 'laysa', 'ma') to negate the entire predication.
Example: 'al-insanu la huwa jablun' (man is not a mountain) is negative because 'la' negates the copula.
3
Apply the Test Using Conditionals
Al-Razi provides a conditional example: 'Whenever man is not a mountain, he is not knowledgeable.' The two parts are negative in wording, but the implication (luzum) between them is affirmed. Therefore the conditional is affirmative. This shows that the logical form (affirmation/negation) depends on the judgment, not the terms.
This test helps identify that a conditional can be affirmative even if its components are negative.
4
Classify Examples
Given a proposition, classify it as saliba or ma'dula. For instance: 'The world is non-eternal' (al-'alamu ghairu qadim) is ma'dula because it affirms non-eternality. 'The world is not eternal' (al-'alamu laysa bi-qadim) is saliba because it denies eternality.
In Islamic theology, the phrase 'the world is non-eternal' (huduth) is a key claim; its logical type affects how it functions in syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Negative Propositions and Non-Existent Subjects
Author discusses the validity of negative propositions when the subject does not exist externally.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish simple negative from 'adula negative propositions and determine their truth conditions regarding subject existence.

Core Idea
Not all negatives about non-existents are false; mental existence suffices for simple negatives.

This passage examines a subtle logical issue: can we truthfully deny something about something that does not exist? The answer, according to Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, depends on the type of negative proposition. Simple negatives are valid if the subject exists in the mind, while 'adula negatives require the subject to exist in external reality. This distinction is crucial for evaluating statements about non-existent entities in theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of negatives
The passage distinguishes between simple negative (saliba basita) and 'adula negative (saliba ma'dula). A simple negative denies the predicate without using a negative term (e.g., 'Zayd is not seeing'). An 'adula negative uses a negative term as predicate (e.g., 'Zayd is not-seeing').
2
Understand the condition for simple negatives
The author states that a simple negative is valid about a non-existent in external reality if the subject exists in the mind, because judgment is a mental relation. Example: 'The phoenix is not a bird' is true if 'phoenix' exists in the mind.
3
Understand the condition for 'adula negatives
An 'adula negative requires the subject to exist externally, because it affirms the negation of a property as a real attribute. Example: 'The phoenix is not-seeing' would be false if the phoenix does not exist externally.
4
Apply the distinction to Islamic examples
Using a kalam example: 'The world is not eternal' is a simple negative about an existing subject. But 'The act before creation is not-obligatory' is a simple negative about a non-existent act (only mental existence). According to Razi, it is valid. An 'adula version would require external existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السلب عن المعدوم في الخارج إذا كان موجودًا في الذهن يصح، لأن الحكم نسبة أمور إلى أمور، والحكم لا يتوقف على وجود المحكوم عليه والمحكوم به في الخارج. والثانية: وهي أن السلب المعدول لا يصح على المعدوم في الخارج لأن الموضوع إذا كان معدومًا فلا يكون محكومًا عليه بالسلب أو الإيجاب. واعلم أن هذا الفرق يظهر بالنظر إلى عادة المشهور الذين لا يعرفون الإيجاب المعدول والسلب البسيط. وأما عند التحقيق، فالسلب يصح على المعدوم في الخارج إذا كان موجودًا في الذهن، لأن الحكم نسبة ذهنية، والنسبة لا تتوقف على الوجود الخارجي. وأما القضية السالبة البسيطة فتصح على المعدوم في الخارج أيضًا، لأنها تحكم بعدم ثبوت شيء لشيء وذلك لا يقتضي وجود الموضوع. وأما القضية المعدولة فتحتاج في صدقها إلى وجود الموضوع في الخارج، لأنها تحكم بثبوت سلب الصفة، وذلك السلب إنما يكون موجودًا إذا كان الموضوع موجودًا. فلهذا لا تصح على المعدوم في الخارج. وقد اختلفوا في هذه المسألة: فمنهم من منع مطلقًا، ومنهم من أجاز. والصحيح ما ذكرناه.
Negation concerning a non-existent in external reality, if it exists in the mind, is valid, because judgment is a relation between matters, and judgment does not depend on the existence of the subject and predicate in external reality. The second [point]: that the 'adula negation is not valid concerning a non-existent in external reality, because if the subject is non-existent, then it is not judged by negation or affirmation. Know that this distinction appears by considering the common practice of those who do not know the 'adula affirmation and simple negation. As for verification, negation is valid concerning a non-existent in external reality if it exists in the mind, because judgment is a mental relation, and the relation does not depend on external existence. As for the simple negative proposition, it is also valid concerning a non-existent in external reality, because it judges the non-establishment of something for something, and that does not require the existence of the subject. As for the 'adula proposition, it requires for its truth the existence of the subject in external reality, because it judges the establishment of the negation of a property, and that negation only exists if the subject exists. Therefore it is not valid concerning a non-existent in external reality. And they differed on this issue: some absolutely forbid, some permit. And the correct is what we have mentioned.
Source pages p. 54

Commentary
This passage is a logical analysis of the validity of negative propositions when the subject does not exist in external reality. The author (Fakhr al-Din al-Razi) explains the conditions under which negative propositions can be true. He distinguishes between simple negatives (saliba basita), 'adula negatives (saliba ma'dula), and the standard view. The key point is that negative propositions can be true about non-existent subjects as long as the subject exists in the mind, because judgment is a mental act. However, 'adula propositions (e.g., 'Zayd is not-seeing') require the subject to exist externally because they affirm the negation of a property, and that negation is a real attribute that cannot be predicated of a non-existent. The author references a dispute: some logicians completely forbid negative propositions about non-existent subjects, while others permit. The correct view, according to the author, is the one he has detailed, which allows simple negatives but restricts 'adula negatives. This analysis is fundamental to understanding how propositions function in logic, especially in arguments involving non-existent entities, such as in theological discussions about the attributes of God or the status of future events.

The Four Quantified Categorical Propositions
Author enumerates the four types of quantified categorical propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a categorical proposition as universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, or particular negative based on its quantifier and quality.

Core Idea
The four quantified propositions are the skeleton of logical reasoning.

In this lesson, we will examine the foundational building blocks of categorical logic: the four quantified propositions. Traditionally known as A, E, I, O, these are the only possible forms of simple categorical statements. Mastering them is essential for evaluating arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Identify quantity and quality
Determine whether the proposition applies to all (universal) or some (particular) members of the subject, and whether it affirms or denies the predicate.
2
Recognize standard quantifier words
Universal affirmative uses 'every' (kull), universal negative uses 'no' (lā shay'), particular affirmative uses 'some' (ba‘ḍ), particular negative uses 'not every' (laysa kull).
The Arabic terms in the source are non-standard but correspond to these.
3
Match to one of the four types
Combine quantity and quality to get one of: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative.
This classification is the basis for all categorical syllogisms.

Commentary
This passage defines the four types of quantified categorical propositions (al-mahsūra). The author begins by stating that quantified propositions are four in number. The first is the universal affirmative (al-mūjiba al-kulliyya), which makes a judgment about all individuals of the subject term. The second is the universal negative (al-sāliba al-kulliyya), which denies the predicate of all individuals. The third is the particular affirmative (al-mūjiba al-juz'iyya), which affirms the predicate of some individuals. The fourth is the particular negative (al-sāliba al-juz'iyya), which denies the predicate of some individuals. The author then discusses the quantifiers (sūr), which are words like 'every' (kull) for universal affirmative, 'no' (lā shay') for universal negative, 'some' (ba‘ḍ) for particular affirmative, and 'not every' (laysa kull) for particular negative. The text explores nuances in the usage of these quantifiers, including their application in conditional statements and the difference between 'some' and 'some not' in negative propositions. The discussion emphasizes the importance of quantifiers in determining the quantity of the subject and the scope of the judgment.

The Four Quantified Propositions and the Dual Sense of 'كل'
Author classifies four quantified propositions and discusses the universal affirmative quantifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four types of quantified propositions and distinguish between essential and existential universal quantifiers.

Core Idea
The quantifier 'كل' can be used essentially (no existential import) or existentially (requires existence).

Quantified propositions are the building blocks of logical reasoning. The word 'كل' (every/all) is not always straightforward: it can be used in two distinct ways. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating the truth of universal claims in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Understand the term 'سور' (quantifier)
The quantifier is the word that indicates the generality or particularity of a judgment, such as 'كل' (all) or 'بعض' (some).
2
Memorize the four quantified propositions
The four types are: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative. They are classified by quantity (universal/particular) and quality (affirmative/negative).
These are the four basic types from which all categorical propositions are formed.
3
Distinguish the two uses of 'كل'
When 'كل' is used according to essence (بحسب الحقيقة), it applies to all individuals of that essence regardless of existence. When used according to existence (بحسب الوجود), it applies only to existing individuals.
Example: 'كل إنسان ناطق' (essential) vs 'كل إنسان في الدار' (existential).
4
Apply the distinction to truth conditions
An essential universal can be true even if the subject class has no members (e.g., 'All humans are rational' remains true even if no humans existed). An existential universal requires at least one existing member.
This distinction affects syllogistic validity and is important in theological arguments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاللفظ الدال على عموم الحكم يسمى سورا. والمحصورة أربعة: الكلية الموجبة، والكلية السالبة، والجزئية الموجبة، والجزئية السالبة. ثم لفظ 'كل' يستعمل تارة بحسب الحقيقة وتارة بحسب الوجود. فإذا قلنا 'كل إنسان ناطق' فإنما نعني به كل ما هو إنسان في الحقيقة. وإذا قلنا 'كل إنسان في الدار' فإنما نعني به كل إنسان موجود في الخارج. والفرق بينهما أن الأول لا يقتضي الوجود والثاني يقتضيه.
The expression indicating the generality of the judgment is called a quantifier. The quantified [propositions] are four: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative. Then the word 'every' is used sometimes according to essence and sometimes according to existence. So when we say 'Every human is rational,' we mean thereby every thing that is a human in essence. And when we say 'Every human is in the house,' we mean thereby every human existing externally. The difference between them is that the first does not entail existence and the second entails it.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage defines the four types of quantified propositions (المحصورة) and clarifies the meaning of the universal quantifier 'كل' (kull). The author first identifies the quantifier (سور) as the word that indicates generality. Then he lists the four types: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, and particular negative. This classification is fundamental in logic for determining the quantity and quality of propositions. Then the author explains that the word 'كل' can be used in two ways: according to essence (بحسب الحقيقة) or according to existence (بحسب الوجود). For example, 'Every human is rational' means that every being that is essentially human (regardless of actual existence) is rational. In contrast, 'Every human is in the house' applies only to humans that actually exist in the external world. The key distinction is that the first usage does not require the subject to exist, while the second does. This nuance is important in logic because it affects the truth conditions of universal propositions and their use in syllogisms.

The Absolute Proposition and Its Truth Conditions
Author explains the absolute proposition and its dependence on particular instances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the truth condition of an absolute proposition and distinguish it from a universal proposition.

Core Idea
The absolute proposition is true if even one instance of the subject has the predicate.

In Islamic logic, propositions are classified by modality and quantity. The absolute proposition is the simplest—it asserts the predicate without modality. But its truth condition is often misunderstood: it does not require universality. This passage clarifies that a single instance suffices, a key distinction for later modal and legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
The passage discusses the absolute proposition (al-qadiyya al-mutlaqa). 'Absolute' here means unrestricted by modality. The example 'Man is a writer' asserts the predicate 'writer' of the subject 'man' without specifying necessity, permanence, or time.
2
Contrast with universal and particular
The author contrasts al-kulliyya (universal) and al-juz'iyya (particular). The absolute's truth depends on the particular, not the universal. Understand that universal requires every individual; particular requires at least one.
3
Test the truth condition with the example
Consider 'Man is a writer'. If no human has ever written, it's false. But if one human (e.g., 'this man') writes, then 'Man is a writer' is true even if not all write. This shows the absolute does not require universality.
4
Analyze the logical reasoning
The author argues that from 'this man is a writer' we cannot infer 'man is a writer' universally. That would be a fallacy of composition. So the absolute is weaker than the universal; it only requires a particular instance.
5
Apply to other Islamic sciences
In usul al-fiqh, an absolute command (al-amr al-mutlaq) like 'Perform the prayer' applies to any act of prayer, not requiring every possible prayer. This distinction matters for legal inference and understanding the scope of rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القضية المطلقة توقف صدقها على صدق الجزئية ولا تتوقف صدقها على صدق الكلية. وكل قضية كذلك فيبين قوة الجزئية. أما الصغرى فالتلخيص الأول: أنا إذا قلنا الإنسان كاتب، فلو لم يكن شخص من أشخاص الناس كاتبا في وقت من الأوقات لكذب ذلك، لأن السلب العام في الكل يستلزم الكذب. وعلى الثاني: أنه من صدق هذا الإنسان لا تصدق أن الإنسان كاتب لأن هذا الإنسان عبارة عن الإنسان المقيد بكونه هذا، والحكم على المحل صدق عداته لكن لا توقف صدق قولنا هذا الإنسان كاتب على صدق الكلية. فإذا لا توقف صدق المطلقة على صدق الكلية.
The absolute proposition: its truth depends on the truth of the particular proposition, and its truth does not depend on the truth of the universal proposition. And every proposition is like that, so the strength of the particular is shown. As for the minor premise, the first summary: if we say 'Man is a writer', then if no individual person among people is a writer at any time, that statement would be false, because universal negation in all entails falsehood. As for the second: from the truth of 'this man [is a writer]' it does not follow that 'man is a writer' [is true], because 'this man' is an expression for man restricted by being this [individual], and the judgment on the locus [applies] to its individuals, but the truth of our statement 'this man is a writer' does not depend on the truth of the universal. Therefore, the truth of the absolute does not depend on the truth of the universal.
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage defines the absolute proposition (al-qadiyya al-mutlaqa) and clarifies its truth conditions. The author argues that the truth of an absolute proposition depends on the truth of a particular proposition (juz'iyya), not on the universal (kulliyya). The example given is 'Man is a writer' (al-insan katib). If no human individual has ever been a writer, the statement is false. But if at least one individual is a writer (e.g., 'this man is a writer'), the absolute proposition is true, even if not all humans write. The key point is that the absolute does not require universal application; a single instance suffices. The author then contrasts this with the universal affirmative, which requires all individuals to have the predicate. This distinction is foundational for modal logic in the Islamic tradition.

Key Takeaway

Truth Condition of the Absolute Proposition
Author defines absolute proposition and proves its truth condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the truth condition of the absolute proposition and explain why it depends on the particular, not the universal, using logical reasoning from the passage.

Core Idea
An absolute proposition requires only one instance to be true, not all.

In logic, not all propositions are universal. The absolute proposition, like 'Man is a writer,' is true even if only one man writes. This lesson unpacks that condition and the logical arguments supporting it, as presented by Fakhr al-Din al-Razi in the Mulakhkhas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition types
Locate the three types discussed: absolute (مطلقة), particular (جزئية), and universal (كلية). The absolute is defined as having no condition of universality.
2
Extract the main claim
The author states: 'The truth of the absolute depends on the truth of the particular, and not on the truth of the universal.' Write this in your own words.
This is the core thesis.
3
Analyze the minor premise proof
The proof uses 'Man is a writer.' If no individual were a writer, the universal would be false. But the truth of 'This man is a writer' does not require all men to be writers. This shows the absolute only needs a particular instance.
This invokes the immediate inference: universal negation entails particular negation.
4
Examine the major premise reasoning
The author argues that the universal being known does not make the particular depend on it. The composite's known attribute does not mean each part has it. So the absolute stands independent of the universal.
This refutes a potential misunderstanding.
5
Review the objection and response
An objector claims that if the essence as such has an attribute, then wherever essence is found, attribute is found. The author responds that the attribute (writing) is found with this individual, so it must be with every part of him. But another objection calls this question-begging.
The discussion hinges on whether the attribute is essential or accidental.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

من أشخاص الناس كاتبا في وقت من الأوقات، لكذب ذلك؛ لأن السلب العالم في الكل يلزم منه سلب الجزئي. وعلى الثاني: أنه ليس من صدق هذا الإنسان لا تصدق أن الإنسان كاتب؟ بل هذا الإنسان عبارة عن الإنسان مع تقييد كونه هذا، فالماصدق على الجمع لا يصدق على كل فرد. لكن لا يتوقف صدق قولنا هذا الإنسان كاتب على صدق الكلية. وأما الكبرى فالتقرير: أن الكلية إذا كانت معلومة والجزئية محمولة بحذف الجزئية، فالمجهول الجزئية: هذه الماهية مع قطع النظر عما عداها من الفرد لما كانت موصوفة بصفة، فأينما حققت تلك الماهية فقد حقق الموصوف بتلك الصفة، وإذا حقق الموصوف بتلك الصفة حققت تلك الصفة، فإنها عين تلك الماهية. لكن يعقب ذلك أن الماهية مجردة عن الصفة؟ لا، لأن الكتابة إنما وجدت مع هذا الإنسان، فلا بد وأن تكون موجودة مع كل واحد من أجزاء هذا الإنسان. وعن الثاني: أن قولكم: الماهية لما كانت موصوفة بتلك الصفة أينما حققت حققت تلك الصفة، مصادرة على المطلوب؛ لأنه لا يعود ذلك إلا إذا ثبت أن الحكم على الماهية من حيث هي تصح العموم في الأسورة المحمولات. منهم من منع لأن اسم من حيث هي لا يكون كليا بل الكلية إنما تفيد عند كونها مقولاف حروف العبر كثرة مع موضوعاته لا نعرض له الحلبة والسود لفظة دالة عن تقدير تلك الكرم فلا يدخل الأنا الموضوع. ومنهم من جوزه. وقبل الموضوع التفصيل لابد من بيان أمرين: السور على هذا التقدير يكون جزء المنطاقية المحمول، فإذا قلنا: زيد بعض الناس، فلا نقول المحمول الناس ولفظة البعض سور، بل نقول المحمول مجموع قولنا: بعض الناس. وأما إذا كان تخفيا استحال دخول السور الموجه الذي يجب أن يثبت عليه لكن يمكن إدخال السور الذي بحسب الأجزاء عليه، كأن يقال: كل هذه الأعضاء أو بعض النجوم. وإذا عرفت ذلك فصول: إذا دخلنا الشعور الذي حسب احتبات على المحمول، فإما أن يكون الموضوع والمحمول متحصن الكليتين، أو المحمول شخصيا والموضوع كليا، أو بالعكس. فالقسم الأول باطل الموحسين: أنا إذا المطوع تجدين أنا إذا في الموجتنا قانات بر كل ذلك الشراح بعضه، فإنما يصح الو اندرج عن ذلك الشخص بنات.
And if you know that, then it is said: The absolute proposition's truth depends on the truth of the particular proposition, and its truth does not depend on the truth of the universal. And every proposition like that is in the power of the particular. As for the minor premise, the first account: that if we say 'Man is a writer,' then if there were no individual person who is a writer at any time, that would be false because universal negation in the whole necessitates negation of the particular. And as for the second: that it is not from the truth of 'This man [is a writer]' that 'Man is a writer' is true? Rather, 'this man' is an expression for man with the restriction of his being 'this', so what is true of the collective is not true of every individual. But the truth of our statement 'This man is a writer' does not depend on the truth of the universal. And as for the major premise, the account: that if the universal is known and the particular is predicated by deleting the particular, then the unknown depends on the known? So the particular is related to the universal like genus? No, because the fact that the composite is known as possessing the attribute does not necessitate that each of its parts possesses it. Then what appears is that the absolute is not true except when the particular is true: this essence, when considered without regard to what is other than it from the individual, since it is described by an attribute, so wherever that essence is realized, the subject described by that attribute is realized, and when the subject described by that attribute is realized, that attribute is realized, for it is the very essence. But that is followed by [the question] that the essence is stripped of the attribute? No, because writing is only found with this man, so it must be found with every part of this man. And as for the second [objection]: that your statement 'the essence, since it is described by that attribute, wherever it is realized, that attribute is realized' is begging the question; because that only holds if it is established that the judgment upon the essence as such is valid for the universality of the predicates in the quantified [propositions]. Some have forbidden because the term 'as such' does not make it universal, rather universality only occurs when it is said upon many of its subjects. The term 'absolute' and 'conditioned' indicate an estimation of that limitation, so it does not enter into the subject. And some have allowed it. And before the detailed discussion, two matters must be clarified: The quantifier on this estimation becomes part of the logical predicate. So if we say 'Zayd is some people,' we do not say the predicate is 'people' and the word 'some' is a quantifier; rather, we say the predicate is the sum of our statement 'some people.' And if it is [something else], it is impossible for the directed quantifier that must be affirmed to enter upon it, but it is possible to enter the quantifier that pertains to the parts upon it, such as saying 'every one of these limbs' or 'some of them.' And if you know that, then sections: if we enter the quantifier that pertains to the predicate, then either the subject and predicate are both universal, or the predicate is particular and the subject is universal, or vice versa. The first division is invalid because of the two established [points]: that if we ... because that only holds when that individual is included under that [species].
Source pages p. 58

Commentary
This passage discusses the truth conditions of the absolute proposition (al-qadiyya al-mutlaqa). The author states that the truth of an absolute proposition depends on the truth of the particular proposition (juz'iyya), not on the truth of the universal (kulliyya). He provides a proof. The minor premise is illustrated with the example 'Man is a writer': if no individual person were a writer at any time, the universal statement would be false because the negation of the whole implies negation of the part. However, the truth of 'This man is a writer' does not require the truth of 'Every man is a writer.' The major premise explains that the universal being known does not make the particular dependent on it in that way, as the composite's being known does not require each part to be known. He then addresses an objection: if the essence as such is described by an attribute, then wherever that essence is found, the attribute is found, but this is questioned because the essence may be considered stripped of the attribute. The author counters that writing is found with this man, so it must be found with every part of him. A further objection claims this begs the question, as it assumes the judgment applies universally. He mentions disagreements about whether the absolute or conditioned term is part of the subject. Finally, he discusses quantifiers entering the predicate, concluding that if both subject and predicate are universal, the proposition is not valid because the individual must be included under the species. The reasoning relies on examples from Islamic logic, using 'man' and 'writer' as standard examples.

The Indeterminate Proposition and Its Truth Condition
Author defines indeterminate proposition and debates quantifier placement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an indeterminate proposition (muhmala) and determine its truth condition relative to the particular.

Core Idea
The indeterminate proposition is true only if its particular is true.

In classical logic, propositions are classified by quantity: universal, particular, and indeterminate. The indeterminate proposition lacks a quantifier. This lesson examines its definition and a key debate about whether a quantifier can be part of the predicate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the indeterminate proposition
Look for a categorical proposition without any quantifier like 'all' or 'some'. For example, 'Human is animal' is indeterminate.
The absence of a quantifier makes it indeterminate in quantity.
2
Apply the truth condition
Check if the corresponding particular proposition (with 'some') is true. If 'Some humans are animals' is true, then 'Human is animal' (indeterminate) is also true.
The author states: 'the indeterminate proposition is not true except when the particular is true'.
3
Analyze the debate over the quantifier in the predicate
Some logicians argue that in 'Zayd is some people', the predicate is the whole 'some people', not 'people' with 'some' as a separate quantifier.
This affects how we understand propositions like 'Every human is every animal' (false) and 'Zayd is some people'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الذي بدت علا أن المحملة لا تصدق إلا عند صدق الجزئية لأن الماهية مع قطع النظر عما عداها من الأفراد لما كانت موصوفة بصفة، فأينما حققت تلك الماهية فقد حقق الموصوف بتلك الصفة. وليس الأمر كذلك في الصورة [؟] لأن الكتابة إنما وجدت مع هذا الإنسان، فلا بد وأن تكون موجودة مع كل واحد من أجزاء هذا الإنسان. وعن الثاني أن قولكم الماهية لما كانت موصوفة بتلك الصفة، أينما حققت تلك الماهية حققت تلك الصفة، مصادرة على المطلوب، لأنه لا يعود ذلك إلا إذا ثبت أن الحكم على الماهية من حيث هي هي، وذلك يتوقف على العموم في الأسوارة. والمحولات منهم من منع لأن اسم من حيث هي لا يكون كليا بل الكلية إنما تفيد عند كونها مقولة على كثيرين مع موضوعاتها، لا نعرض له الحلية والسواد لفظة دالة عن تقرير تلك الكلية فلا يدخل في الموضوع. ومنهم من جوزه. وقبل التفصيل لابد من بيان أمرين: السور على هذا التقدير يكون جزء المنطوق المحمول، فإذا قلنا زيد بعض الناس فلا نقول المحمول الناس ولفظة البعض سور، بل نقول المحمول مجموع قولنا بعض الناس. وإذا كان تخفيا استحال دخول السور الموجه الذي يجب أن يثبت عليه لكن يمكن إدخال السور الذي بحسب الأجزاء عليه كأن تقول كل هذه الأعضاء أو بعض هذه الأعضاء. وإذا عرفت ذلك فصول: إذا دخلنا السور الذي بحسب الإجزاء على المحمول فالموضوع والمحمول إما أن يكونا كليين أو المحمول شخصيا والموضوع كليا أو بالعكس فالقسم الأول باطل لأنك إذا قلت كل إنسان كل حيوان، فذلك كذب، وإنما يصح إذا اندرج عن ذلك الشخص [؟]
Then what appears is that the indeterminate proposition is not true except when the particular is true, because the essence, disregarding its individuals, when it is described with a property, then wherever that essence is realized, the thing described with that property is realized. And it is not like that in the form [؟] because writing is only found with this human, so it must be found with every one of the parts of this human. As for the second [objection], that your statement 'the essence, when it is described with that property, wherever the essence is realized, that property is realized' is begging the question, because it only follows if it is established that the judgment is upon the essence as such, and that depends on universality in the quantifiers. Some of the [scholars] prevented [the quantifier from entering the predicate] because the term 'as such' is not universal; rather universality only obtains when it is predicated of many with their subjects; we do not attend to the [؟] and blackness is an expression indicating that universality, so it does not enter into the subject. And some allowed it. Before the detailed discussion, two matters must be clarified: the quantifier on this estimation becomes part of the literal predicate. So when we say 'Zayd is some of the people', we do not say the predicate is 'people' and the word 'some' is a quantifier; rather we say the predicate is the composite of our saying 'some people'. If it is implicit, the entry of the directed quantifier that must be affirmed upon it is impossible, but it is possible to enter the quantifier that is according to the parts, like you say 'all these members or some of these members'. And when you know that, then sections: if we enter the quantifier that is according to the parts upon the predicate, then the subject and predicate are either both universal, or the predicate is particular and the subject universal, or vice versa. So the first division is false because if you say 'every human is every animal', that is false; it is only valid if [؟] from that person [؟].
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage defines the indeterminate proposition (al-muhmala) and discusses a key debate about quantifiers (aswār). The author begins by stating that an indeterminate proposition is true only when its particular counterpart is true. The reasoning is that if a property belongs to an essence considered in itself, then wherever that essence is found, the property must be present. However, this is not analogous to a case like 'writing' being attached to a specific individual, because writing does not extend to all parts of that individual. The author then responds to an objection: the claim that wherever the essence is realized the property is realized is a petitio principii, because it assumes that the judgment is upon the essence as such, which requires universality—the very point in dispute. Some scholars deny that a quantifier can become part of the predicate, arguing that terms like 'as such' do not imply universality; universality only occurs when the term is predicated of many. Others allow it. Before delving into details, two clarifications are needed: (1) if the quantifier is taken as part of the predicate, then in 'Zayd is some of the people', the predicate is the whole 'some people', not just 'people' with 'some' as a separate quantifier; (2) when the quantifier is implicit, entering a directed quantifier (e.g., 'all') is impossible, but a quantifier according to parts (e.g., 'all these members') can be entered. Finally, the author categorizes the cases when the quantifier is applied to the predicate: both subject and predicate universal, subject universal and predicate particular (or vice versa), and both particular. The first division (both universal) is declared false because 'every human is every animal' is false; it would only be valid if something like inclusion holds.

سور
كلي

Key Takeaway
The truth of an indeterminate proposition hinges on the truth of its particular counterpart.
