Classification of Necessity and Possibility in Modal Propositions
Author classifies types of necessity and divides possibility into general and special.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between absolute necessity and conditional necessity, and between general possibility and special possibility, using examples from Islamic theology and logic.

Core Idea
Modal distinctions determine how necessary or possible a predicate is for a subject.

In this passage, the author breaks down necessity into absolute and conditional, and possibility into general and special. These modal classifications are not just theoretical; they affect how we understand propositions about God, creation, and human actions. For example, 'God is knowing' is absolutely necessary, while 'every body is susceptible to accidents' is conditional. Understanding these divisions helps you avoid logical errors in theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main modal categories
The passage divides modal propositions into those concerning necessity (darura) and those concerning possibility (imkan). Notice that necessity has two types: absolute (mutlaqa) and conditional (bishart al-wasf). Possibility also has two: general ('am) and special (khas).
2
Distinguish absolute from conditional necessity
Absolute necessity means the predicate is inseparable from the subject by essence, without any condition. Example: 'God is knowing'. Conditional necessity requires the subject to be described by a certain quality for the predicate to be necessary. Example: 'Man is an animal' – this is only true if man is considered rational animal.
The author warns that many people mistakenly treat 'man is an animal' as absolute necessity.
3
Understand general and special possibility
General possibility (al-imkan al-'am) is defined as the negation of necessity. It includes the necessary (if negating non-existence) and the impossible (if negating existence), as well as the special possible. Special possibility (al-imkan al-khas) is the equal possibility of existence and non-existence, excluding the necessary and impossible.
The author clarifies that general possibility is broader than special possibility.
4
Apply the distinctions to given examples
Now test your understanding: classify 'Every body is susceptible to accidents' – is it absolute or conditional? Classify 'It is possible that the sky will fall' – is it general or special possibility?
Use the definitions from the passage.

And it is better that its derivation is from 'ilm, for this meaning is in the intention of the Arab whose absolute expression is accompanied by its meaning, like the absolute necessity and by condition of the description of the subject and according to time, absolute or limited. And this is empty of the necessity obtained for the external cause, except that it is realized from necessity by condition that it is free from the three considerations. So know that the lack occurs to it in the state of its existence, and it may be considered in relation to future attributes and from the people.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion on modal propositions, specifically classifying types of necessity (darura) and possibility (imkan). The author begins by distinguishing absolute necessity (al-darura al-mutlaqa) where the predicate is inseparable from the subject by essence, giving the example 'God is knowing'—where the attribute of knowledge is necessary for God's essence. He contrasts this with conditional necessity, where the predicate holds under a condition, such as 'every body is susceptible to accidents' (kull jism qabil li-l-'arad). The body itself does not necessitate accidents, but given its nature, accidents are necessary. He then critiques the common example 'man is an animal' (al-insan hayawan), noting that people mistakenly treat it as a primary premise, though it actually requires the condition that man is a rational animal; otherwise, the animality is not absolutely necessary. He further discusses necessity by description of the subject (bi-shart wasf al-mawdu'), where the predicate is necessary only if the subject is described by a certain quality. Then he shifts to possibility (imkan), dividing it into two: special possibility (al-mumkin al-khas) which is equally possible to exist or not exist, and general possibility (al-mumkin al-'am) which negates necessity of either existence or non-existence. The general possibility includes the necessary (if negating non-existence) and the impossible (if negating existence) along with the special possible. The author ends with a note on terminology and a reference to the Shaykh (likely Ibn Sina), emphasizing that general possibility is defined by negation of necessity and is broader than special possibility.

Key Terms
الضرورة المطلقة (al-darura al-mutlaqa): Absolute necessity; the predicate is necessary for the subject without any condition, e.g., 'God is knowing'. [Note: In earlier logic, absolute necessity often meant eternal necessity; here it includes essential predication.]
بشرط الوصف (bi-shart al-wasf): Necessity conditioned on the subject being described by a certain quality; e.g., man is animal only if considered as rational animal. [Note: This is a standard distinction in modal logic; no significant shift.]
الإمكان العام (al-imkan al-'am): General possibility; negation of necessity, either of existence or non-existence, thus including the necessary and impossible as limits. [Note: In later texts, al-imkan al-'am sometimes refers to contingency (non-necessity) as a genus; here it is defined as salb al-darura.]
الإمكان الخاص (al-imkan al-khas): Special possibility; equal possibility of existence and non-existence, excluding the necessary and impossible. [Note: Stable across the tradition.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of absolute necessity (al-darura al-mutlaqa) according to the passage?
A) Every body is susceptible to accidents.
B) Man is an animal.
C) God is knowing.
D) It is possible that the sky will fall.

Key Takeaway
Modal logic classifies propositions by necessity and possibility; absolute necessity is unconditional, while conditional necessity and general/special possibility refine our understanding of predication.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Certainty in Universal vs Particular Propositions
Author argues universal propositions require necessity for certainty, but particular propositions do not.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the epistemic conditions required for certainty in universal vs particular propositions.

Core Idea
Certainty in universal propositions demands necessity; sense perception suffices only for particulars.

This passage explores why universal propositions cannot be known with certainty through sense perception alone, while particular propositions can. The author argues that the intellect requires metaphysical necessity to be certain of a universal claim, because sense perception cannot cover all instances. This is a cornerstone of Islamic epistemology, influencing how scholars assess certainty in kalam and usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of propositions
Look for where the author discusses 'universal proposition' (qadiyya kulliyya) and 'particular proposition' (qadiyya juz'iyya). Notice that the conditions for certainty differ between them.
2
Understand the limitation of sense perception
The author states that sense perception (hiss) cannot grasp all particular instances, past, present, and future. This means it cannot ground a universal judgment.
Even if sense could grasp all existing instances, it would not cover future ones, so universality requires more.
3
Recognise the necessity condition for universals
The intellect can only be certain of a universal proposition if the predicate is necessary (daruriyya) for the subject. This necessity ensures the judgment holds for all instances, not just observed ones.
Necessity here is metaphysical: the predicate is inseparable from the subject.
4
Contrast with particular propositions
For particular propositions, sense perception can directly observe an instance, providing certainty without necessity. The author contrasts this with the universal case.
The example of sunrise and sunset illustrates that without belief in necessity, one cannot be certain of the universal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاذن كل الممكن وقوعه لا يمكن القطع بأحد طرفيه إلا بالحصر لكن الحس لا يفي بإدراك جمع الجزئيات الداخلة في الوجود ولو في به أيضا لم يكف ذلك كون القضية كلية لانا اذا قلنا كل لا نعني به انما دخل في الوجود من الحوادث بل نعني به كلما وجد ومعلوم أن ذلك تمالا لكن الاحساس به نعلم أن العقل لا يمكن من الجزم بالقضية الكلية إلا إذا كانت ضرورية فأما القضية الجزئية فان العقل يجزم بها وإن لم تكن ضرورية لاستفادة الحس إفادة الجزئي والحكماء أولى الناس بهذه المقالة لاعتقادهم أن التي لا يعرف وجوده إلا من جهة العلم به... واما الذي يشك فيه من حديث الشروق والغروب فمن لم يعتقد وجوب الشروق والغروب للكواكب لا يقطع بأن كل كوكب موصوف بالشروق والغروب واما اذا قلنا الان من تلك الحمل حقيقة ومفهوم في انفس الحقائق فضوان من مبادئه الله منكم التوجه المذكر الا... ان ذلك السلس حاتم او غير دائم او مشروط... نعم الاحوال كلها على هذا التقدير يصدق ان القضية من المان
For every possible occurrence cannot be decided on one of its two sides except by enumeration, but sense perception cannot grasp all particular instances that enter into existence, and even if it could, that would not suffice for the proposition to be universal. For when we say 'every', we do not mean only what has entered into existence from events, but we mean 'every that exists'. And it is known that this is [complete?] but by sense perception we know that the intellect cannot be certain of the universal proposition unless it is necessary. As for the particular proposition, the intellect can be certain of it even if it is not necessary, because sense perception provides knowledge of the particular. And the wise are the most appropriate people for this statement because they believe that that whose existence is not known except through knowledge... And as for what one doubts from the example of sunrise and sunset, whoever does not believe in the necessity of sunrise and sunset for the planets cannot be certain that every planet is characterized by sunrise and sunset. And if we say now that the judgment is [true] in reality and concept... that the [judgment] is perpetual or not perpetual or conditional... Yes, all states according to this estimation verify that the proposition is [universal].
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage discusses the epistemological conditions for certainty in universal and particular propositions. The author argues that for universal propositions, sense perception is insufficient to ground certainty because it cannot grasp all instances, whether past, present, or future. Therefore, the intellect can only be certain of a universal proposition if it is necessary (i.e., the predicate necessarily belongs to the subject). In contrast, for particular propositions, sense perception can provide certainty without necessity because it directly perceives a specific instance. The author then addresses an objection concerning the example of sunrise and sunset: some might doubt the universality of 'every planet rises and sets' because they do not believe it is necessary. The author implies that if one does not believe in the necessity, one cannot be certain of the universal proposition. The passage concludes that under this estimation, the proposition can still be considered universal in a certain sense. This discussion is central to the epistemology of propositions, showing that for universal knowledge, necessity is required, while particular knowledge can rely on sense perception.

جزئية

Why Universal Propositions Need Necessity
Author argues that certainty in universal propositions requires necessity, not sensation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why empirical induction alone cannot yield certainty for universal propositions and identify the role of necessity in universal judgments.

Core Idea
Without necessity, a universal proposition cannot be certain, no matter how many particulars you examine.

The author addresses a fundamental epistemological question: can sensation alone give us certain universal knowledge? He argues that it cannot, because universality implies a conditional covering all possible individuals, not just existing ones. Only necessary propositions, whose truth is independent of empirical verification, can yield certainty. This lesson unpacks that argument and its implications for Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key claim about universal propositions
The author states that the intellect cannot be certain of a universal proposition unless it is necessary. Locate this claim in the Arabic text and understand why it is important.
The claim is in the middle of the passage: 'But the intellect cannot be certain of the universal proposition unless it is necessary.'
2
Distinguish between exhaustive enumeration and necessity
The author contrasts two methods: al-ḥaṣr (exhaustive enumeration) and necessity. Note that even complete empirical enumeration does not make a proposition universal because 'every' means 'whenever an individual exists, the judgment holds' – a conditional that goes beyond existing particulars.
This is the core logical insight: universality is conditional, not extensional.
3
Understand the conditional nature of the universal quantifier
The author says: 'when we say 'every' we do not mean that we have encompassed all individuals that exist, but rather we mean whenever an individual exists, the judgment is such.' This shows that universality involves a conditional necessity, not just a summary of observed cases.
This is a key distinction between logical universality and empirical generalization.
4
Apply the argument to the sunrise/sunset example
The author gives an example: if someone does not believe that sunrise and sunset are necessary for planets, they cannot be certain that every planet has sunrise and sunset. This illustrates that without necessity, we cannot assert a universal judgment with certainty.
The example shows the practical epistemological consequence of the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاذن كل الممكن وقوعه لا يمكن القطع بأحد طرفيه إلا بالحصر. لكن الحس لا يفي بإدراك جميع الجزئيات الداخلة في الوجود. ولو وفي به أيضاً لم يكف ذلك كون القضية كلية، لأنا إذا قلنا كل لا نعني به أننا أحطنا بما دخل في الوجود من الأفراد، بل نعني به كلما وجد فرد فالحكم كذا. ومعلوم أن ذلك لا يمكن. لكن العقل لا يمكنه الجزم بالقضية الكلية إلا إذا كانت ضرورية. فأما القضية الجزئية فإن العقل يجزم بها وإن لم تكن ضرورية، لاستفادة الحس إفادة الجزئي. والحكماء أولى الناس بهذه المقالة لاعتقادهم أن التي لا يعرف وجوده إلا من جهة الحس لا يمكن القطع به. والذي يحيى به شيخ من حديث الشروق والغروب: من لم يعتقد وجوب الشروق والغروب للكواكب لا يقطع بأن كل كوكب موصوف بالشروق والغروب.
Thus, every possible occurrence cannot be decisively known in either of its two sides except by exhaustive enumeration. But sensation does not suffice to perceive all particulars existing in the world. And even if it did suffice, that would not make the proposition universal, because when we say 'every' we do not mean that we have encompassed all individuals that exist, but rather we mean whenever an individual exists, the judgment is such. And it is known that this cannot be. But the intellect cannot be certain of the universal proposition unless it is necessary. As for the particular proposition, the intellect can be certain of it even if it is not necessary, because sensation provides the particular. The philosophers are the most entitled to this statement because they believe that what is known only through sensation cannot be certain. And what the sheikh revived regarding sunrise and sunset: one who does not believe in the necessity of sunrise and sunset for the planets does not assert that every planet is described by sunrise and sunset.
Source pages p. 68

Commentary
This passage presents an epistemological argument about the nature of universal and particular propositions. The author contends that certainty in a universal proposition is impossible unless it is necessary, because empirical induction (al-hasr) cannot cover all particular instances. Even if sensation could perceive all existing particulars, that still would not guarantee universality, as the universal quantifier 'every' implies a conditional covering all possible individuals, not just those existing. Therefore, only necessary propositions, whose truth is independent of empirical verification, can yield certainty. In contrast, particular propositions can be known with certainty through sensation alone, even if they are contingent. The author notes that the philosophers (al-hukama') endorse this view, as they hold that knowledge derived solely from sense perception cannot be certain. An example is given of someone who does not believe that sunrise and sunset are necessary for celestial bodies; such a person cannot be certain that every planet has sunrise and sunset. This illustrates that empirical generalization without necessity falls short of certainty.

Key Terms

ضروري

حصر

Types of Modal Propositions: Simple and Compound
Author classifies modal propositions into simple and compound, then details two types of permanence modality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish simple from compound modal propositions and identify the two key modalities: al-dā'ima and al-urfiyya al-ʿāmma.

Core Idea
Modal propositions tell how the predicate relates to the subject—permanently or necessarily.

In this lesson, we explore how logicians classify modal propositions. Razi breaks them into simple and compound, then explains two fundamental types of permanence modality. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for evaluating the truth conditions of statements in Islamic sciences, such as in kalam arguments about divine attributes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two pairs of simple modalities
From the text, note that simple modalities are from two aspects: permanence vs. non-permanence, and necessity vs. non-necessity. This gives four basic categories.
Compound modalities result from combining these simple ones.
2
Understand al-dā'ima (permanent)
A proposition is dā'ima if the predicate persists as long as the subject's essence exists. Example: 'Every human is essentially breathing'—breathing is a necessary concomitant of human essence.
The predicate must hold as long as the subject's essence remains.
3
Grasp al-urfiyya al-ʿāmma (general customary)
Here the predicate holds as long as a description of the subject persists, provided that description is permanent with the essence (either identical to essence or necessary for it). The passage gives three subcategories: type, primary, and customary.
The predicate is not strictly necessary but follows from the subject's description.
4
Compare dā'ima and ʿurfiyya ʿāmma
In dā'ima, the predicate holds for the entire duration of the subject's essence. In ʿurfiyya ʿāmma, it holds only as long as a specific description holds, even if that description is essential. The subcategories show different grounds for the predicate's persistence.
This distinction is important for evaluating logical necessity vs. customary regularity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاعلم أن الموجّهات عبارة عن بيان كيفية القصة، وهي قد تكون بسيطة وقد تكون مركبة. فالبسيطة من وجهين: الدوام واللادوام، والضرورة واللا ضرورة. والمركبة ما يتركب عنها. وأما أنواع الجهات حقيقة الدوام واللادوام فأربعة: فالأولى التي بين فيها أن المحمول دام بدوام الذات التي في الموضوع بالحقيقة وهي الدائمة. ثم إن كان وصف الموضوع دائماً بدوام الذات إما أنه هي أو لأنه لازم لها، فالمحمول أيضاً حاتماً بدوام وصف الموضوع، وهي العرفية العامة. وتندرج فيها أقسام ثلاثة: لأن المحمول الذي بدوام وصف الموضوع إما أن لا يعدم وجد الموضوع بدوام وجود الموضوع نوعه كقولنا: كل إنسان متنفس بالحقيقة، أو يكون أولياً كقولنا: كل إنسان له بداية، أو يكون عادياً كقولنا: كل نار حارة.
Know that modal propositions are expressions that explain the modality (kayfiyya) of the proposition. They may be simple or compound. The simple are from two aspects: permanence and non-permanence, necessity and non-necessity. The compound is what is composed from them. As for the types of modalities in essence of permanence and non-permanence, they are four: The first is that in which it is explained that the predicate persisted with the persistence of the essence that is in the subject in reality, and it is the permanent (al-dā'ima). Then if the description of the subject is permanent with the permanence of the essence, either because it is the essence or because it is necessary for it, then the predicate is also necessarily (persisting) with the permanence of the description of the subject, and it is the general customary (al-ʿurfiyya al-ʿāmma). It includes three divisions: because the predicate that is with the permanence of the description of the subject either does not cease from the existence of the subject with the permanence of the existence of its type, like our saying: 'Every human is essentially breathing'; or it is primary, like our saying: 'Every human has a beginning'; or it is customary, like our saying: 'Every fire is hot.'
Source pages p. 69

Commentary
This passage defines modal propositions (al-muwajjahāt) and classifies them into simple and compound. The simple modalities are based on two pairs: permanence (al-dawām) vs. non-permanence (al-lā-dawām), and necessity (al-ḍarūra) vs. non-necessity (al-lā-ḍarūra). Compound modalities result from combining these. The author then explains the types of modalities concerning actual permanence (al-dawām) and non-permanence (al-lā-dawām). There are four kinds. The first is al-dā'ima (the permanent), where the predicate persists as long as the subject's essence exists. An example: 'Every human is essentially breathing'—breathing is a necessary concomitant of human essence. The second is 'al-urfiyya al-ʿāmma' (the general customary), where the predicate persists as long as the subject is described by a certain attribute, provided that attribute is permanent with the subject's essence, either because the attribute is the essence itself or is necessary for it. This type includes three subcategories: (a) the predicate does not cease as long as the subject's type exists (e.g., 'Every human is breathing'—breathing continues as long as human type exists); (b) primary (awwalī), e.g., 'Every human has a beginning'—having a beginning is a primary attribute; (c) customary (ʿādī), e.g., 'Every fire is hot'—heat is a customary concomitant, not strictly necessary. These distinctions are important for evaluating the truth conditions of modal statements in logical reasoning.

Key Terms
موجّهة
دائمة
عرفية عامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) Every human is breathing.

Key Takeaway
Permanence modality distinguishes between essential persistence (dā'ima) and persistence based on description (ʿurfiyya ʿāmma), which includes type, primary, and customary subcategories.

Necessary vs Accidental Conditionals
Author classifies conditional propositions and types of implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a necessary conditional (luzumiyya) and an accidental conditional (ittifaqiyya), and identify the type of implication in a necessary conditional.

Core Idea
Not all conditionals are logically necessary; some are accidental.

After introducing conditional propositions, the author now splits them into two kinds based on the connection between antecedent and consequent. This distinction is vital for evaluating arguments that use 'if-then' statements, as only necessary conditionals guarantee a logical inference. The passage also lists possible causal relationships within necessary conditionals, showing the richness of logical implication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of conditionals
Look for the key terms 'لزومية' (necessary) and 'اتفاقية' (accidental). The criterion is whether the antecedent by itself necessitates the consequent.
2
Understand the distinction criterion
The distinction is based on self-necessitation. If the antecedent logically implies the consequent by its own meaning, it's necessary; if the connection is coincidental, it's accidental.
3
List the kinds of implication in necessary conditionals
The author mentions five types: cause, effect, accident, correlative, and effect of cause. Each represents a different relationship between antecedent and consequent.
4
Note the epistemic status of implication
The implication may be self-evident (بديهي) or require proof. This affects whether the conditional can be used as a premise without further justification.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثاني في إحكام الشروط في المتصلة هي التي توجب على اللازم أو تستحصل قضية عند أخرى ثم إن مقدمها إن لزم لذاته أن يتبعه الثاني كانت لزومية وإلا كانت اتفاقية. والملازمة في اللزومية قد تكون علة للأمر أو معلولة له أو عارضة أو مضايفة أو معلولة العلة. وقد يكون اللازم من كل واحد من هذه الأقسام بديهياً وقد لا يكون.
The second section is about the rulings of conditions in the conditional [proposition]. They are that which necessitate the consequent, or [they] obtain a proposition upon another. Then its antecedent, if by itself it necessitates that the second follow it, it is a necessary [conditional]; otherwise it is accidental. The implication in the necessary [conditional] may be a cause for the matter, or an effect of it, or an accident, or a correlative, or an effect of the cause. And the necessary [conditional] may from each of these divisions be self-evident or not.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage introduces the second part of the discussion on conditional propositions (المتصلة). The author distinguishes between necessary (لزومية) and accidental (اتفاقية) conditionals. In a necessary conditional, the antecedent logically implies the consequent; in an accidental one, the connection is coincidental. The implication (الملازمة) in necessary conditionals can be of various types: the antecedent may be the cause of the consequent, the effect, an accident (non-essential attribute), a correlative (like father-son), or the effect of a cause. Furthermore, the implication may be self-evident (بديهي) or not, meaning it may require proof.

Key Terms
المتصلة
اللزومية
الاتفاقية
الملازمة
العارضة
المضايفة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) اتفاقية
B) لزومية
C) منفصلة
D) حمليّة

Key Takeaway
Necessary conditionals are those where the antecedent logically implies the consequent, and the implication can be of various causal types, each possibly self-evident or requiring proof.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Definition and Types of Conditional Propositions
Author defines conditional and classifies its types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a conjunctive conditional as luzumiyya or ittifaqiyya and identify the type of entailment based on the passage's criteria.

Core Idea
A conditional's necessity depends on an intrinsic relation, not on the truth of its parts.

This passage from al-Mulakhkhas introduces the second section on conditional propositions. It defines the conjunctive conditional and provides a systematic classification that is foundational for later logical analysis. Understanding this classification is crucial for evaluating arguments that use conditionals, especially in Islamic sciences where legal and theological reasoning often relies on conditional statements.

Guided Path
1
Define the conditional
Identify the definition: the conjunctive (muttasila) is a proposition that necessitates or negates the occurrence of one proposition at another. Note the two parts: antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali).
The definition focuses on the relation between propositions, not their individual truth.
2
Classify as luzumiyya or ittifaqiyya
Determine if the consequent follows from the antecedent 'in itself' (li-dhatihi). If the connection is intrinsic, it is luzumiyya (necessary); if accidental, it is ittifaqiyya.
The passage states: 'if it is such that the consequent follows it in itself, it is necessary, otherwise it is accidental.'
3
Identify the type of entailment
For luzumiyya conditionals, the passage lists four types of relations: antecedent as cause of consequent, consequent as cause of antecedent, correlatives (mutadha'ifan), or consequent as effect of a cause of the antecedent.
These are the possible intrinsic connections that make a conditional necessary.
4
Examine the particle
Note the conditional particle used (in, idha, kullama) and its strength in indicating entailment. The passage explains that these particles indicate the state (existence/non-existence) of the antecedent, while the consequent indicates the connection.
The particles do not alone determine necessity; the intrinsic relation does.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الثاني في أحكام القضايا الشرطية. المتصلة هي التي توجب أو تنتفي حصول قضية عند أخرى. ثم إن مقدمها إن كان لذاته يتبعه الثاني كانت لزومية وإلا فاتفاقية. والملازوم في اللزومية قد يكون علة للألزم أو معلولا له أو مضايفا أو معلولا لعلة. وقد يكون اللزوم من كل واحد من هذه الأقسام بديهيا وقد لا يكون. ثم ههنا ثلاثة أبحاث: أحدها أن تكون لفظة 'إن' شديدة الدلالة على التلازم أو ضعيفة. وثانيها أن تكون 'إذا' متوسطة. وثالثها أن تكون 'كلما' دالة على التلازم دائما. ولما كانت هذه الألفاظ صالحة للأمرين في المقدم دلت على الوضع فقط من حيث إنه كان أو ليس، والتالي على الارتباط. فجميع الشرط والجزاء أخرجاها عن أن يكونا قضية. وإذا كان كذلك لم يجب كون واحد منهما من حيث هو هو في المتصلة. نعم إذا نظر إليهما من الخارج كان المطلوب. والمطلوب في المتصلة هو حكم التلازم. فإذا كان المقدم علة للتالي أو التالي علة للمقدم أو بينهما تضايف أو علية من طرف واحد، فهي لزومية. وإلا فاتفاقية.
The second section on the rules of conditional propositions. The conjunctive is that which necessitates or negates the occurrence of a proposition at another. Then its antecedent, if it is such that the consequent follows it in itself, it is necessary, otherwise it is accidental. The entailment in the necessary may be a cause for the necessary, or an effect of it, or correlative, or an effect of a cause. And the entailment in each of these divisions may be evident or not. Then there are three investigations: one that the word 'in' is strong in indicating the entailment or weak. The second that 'idha' is intermediate. The third that 'kullama' indicates constant entailment. And since these particles are suitable for both matters in the antecedent, they indicate only the situation as to whether it was or not, and the consequent indicates the connection. So the entire condition and the consequent are removed from being a proposition. And if that is so, it is not necessary for one of them, in itself, to be in the conjunctive. Yes, if they are considered externally, the requirement is. And the requirement in the conjunctive is the ruling of entailment. So if the antecedent is a cause for the consequent, or the consequent is a cause for the antecedent, or there is correlation between them, or causality from one side, then it is necessary. Otherwise it is accidental.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage opens the second section of the chapter on conditional propositions. It defines the conjunctive conditional (al-muttasila) as a proposition that asserts or negates the obtaining of one proposition upon another. The author then classifies conditionals into two types: luzumiyya (necessary) and ittifaqiyya (accidental), based on whether the connection between antecedent and consequent is intrinsic or coincidental. Within necessary conditionals, the entailment (luzum) can be of four kinds: the antecedent is a cause for the consequent, the consequent is a cause for the antecedent, they are correlatives (mutadha'ifan), or the consequent is an effect of a cause of the antecedent. Each of these may be obvious (badihi) or not. Next, the author mentions three investigations concerning the particles used: whether 'in' indicates strong or weak entailment, 'idha' being intermediate, and 'kullama' indicating constant entailment. These particles only indicate the state of the antecedent (existence or non-existence) whereas the consequent indicates the connection. Hence, the condition and the consequent together are not considered separate propositions but parts of a conditional. The core of the conditional is the ruling of entailment. This classification is crucial for understanding later discussions on syllogisms involving conditionals.

Key Terms
متصلة
لزومية
اتفاقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, in a luzumiyya conditional, if the antecedent is a cause for the consequent, what is the logical relation?
A) The antecedent is an effect of the consequent.
B) The consequent follows necessarily from the antecedent.
C) The connection is accidental.
D) The antecedent and consequent are identical.

Key Takeaway
Conditional propositions are classified into necessary (luzumiyya) and accidental (ittifaqiyya) based on whether the consequent follows intrinsically from the antecedent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Task 1: Classify the following conditional as luzumiyya or ittifaqiyya and determine the type of entailment: 'If the month of Ramadan begins, then fasting is obligatory.' Explain your reasoning using the categories from the passage.

Third Type of Disjunctive: Exclusive Disjunction
Author enumerates five types of disjunctive propositions with examples and rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a third-type disjunctive proposition and verify its truth condition: both parts cannot be true simultaneously.

Core Idea
Third-type disjunction: both parts cannot be true together, but both can be false.

In this passage, the author introduces the third type of disjunctive proposition with the example 'stone or tree'. We will examine the ruling that both parts cannot be true at the same time and understand the proof given through conversion and contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjuncts
Find the two parts of the disjunction in the example. Here they are 'stone' and 'tree'.
The disjuncts are the two options connected by 'or'.
2
Understand the ruling
The author states that it is impossible for both parts to be true together. This means the disjunction is exclusive (one or the other, but not both).
This is known in later logic as 'mani'at al-jam'' (preventing conjunction).
3
Follow the proof
The author argues: if stone is true, then tree must be true (because tree is supposedly more general), leading to both being true, which is a contradiction. Thus, both cannot be true.
The reasoning relies on the relationship between the terms; here the example may be flawed, but the logical principle stands.
4
Apply to Islamic examples
Think of a fiqh ruling: 'This food is either halal or haram.' Both cannot be true simultaneously, fulfilling the third-type condition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مثال الثالث: هذا إما أن يكون حجرا أو شجرا، فإن الأمر هذا لأنه إما أن يكون حجرا أو لا يكون، والأحجار من الحر. فقد وضعنا مقابلة الحر الشجر الذي هو أعم من اللاحجر. وحكمها استحالة صدق الجزئين معا؟؟ فلذلك الأول: فإذا صدق أحد الجزئين صدق الآخر؟؟ لأنه أخص منه. فإذا صدق الحجر مع صدق الشجر لزم صدق الحجر والشجر معا هن؟؟ خلف. ولما الثاني: فلأنه لو كان كلما كذب الحجر صدق الشجر كان البحر ماويا، وكان أخص منه هذا خلف. مثال الرابع: هذا الشيء إما أن لا يكون حجرا وإما أن لا يكون شجرا، وتقديره: إما أن لا يكون حجرا وإما أن لا يكون شجرا. لأن اللاشجرية أعم من اللاحجرية. فإذا وضعنا مقام الحجر اللاشجر فقد جعلنا المنفصلة من الشكل ولازم نقضه. وحكمه امتناع اجتماع الحكمين على الكذب وإمكان اجتماعهما على الصدق. لما الأول: فلأنه حين كذب أنه ليس حجرا لزم كذب أنه ليس شجر؟؟ لزم كذب ذلك؟؟ وأما الثاني: فلأنه لو لزم من صدق أنه ليس حجرا صدق أنه ليس شجر لكان قولنا ليس حجر مساويا لقولنا ليس شجر وقد كان أعم منه، هذا خلف. وأما الخامس: فالطرفان يفتح اجتماعهما على الصدق والكذب معا، لأن الطرفين الإضافيين كان واحد منهما أعم من الآخر من وجه وأخص من وجه آخر، مع أن يوجدا معا وأن يوجد كل واحد منهما مع عدم الآخر، فلا يكون الاجتماع منعت ولا الخلو أيضا.
Third example: This is either a stone or a tree, for the matter is that it is either a stone or not, and stones are from trees? We have placed in opposition to stone the tree which is more general than non-stone. Its ruling is the impossibility of both parts being true together? Therefore the first: if one of the two parts is true, the other is true? because it is more specific than it. So if stone is true with tree being true, then stone and tree together are true? contradiction. As for the second: because if whenever stone is false tree is true, then sea would be watery, and it would be more specific than it, contradiction. Fourth example: This thing is either not a stone or not a tree, and its estimation: either it is not a stone or it is not a tree. Because non-tree is more general than non-stone. So if we put in place of stone the non-stone, we have made the disjunctive of the form and its necessary implication. Its ruling is the impossibility of both judgments being false together and the possibility of both being true together. As for the first: because when it is false that it is not a stone, it follows that it is false that it is not a tree? it follows that falsehood? And as for the second: because if it followed from the truth that it is not a stone that it is not a tree, then our statement 'not stone' would be equivalent to 'not tree' but it was more general, contradiction. As for the fifth: the two parts allow both truth and falsity together, because the two relative parts are such that one is more general than the other in one respect and more specific in another, with the possibility that they exist together or each exists without the other, so it neither prohibits conjunction nor disjunction.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage classifies disjunctive propositions (munfasila) into types based on the relationship between the two parts (jur'an). The third type is a real disjunction where the two parts are mutually exclusive and exhaustive? Actually the text describes it as 'impossibility of both being true' but the example 'stone or tree' is not mutually exclusive? The author says 'it is either a stone or a tree' and claims that because stone implies tree? This is confusing. The fourth type is 'either not stone or not tree' where both parts can be true together (since something can be neither stone nor tree) but cannot both be false? The fifth type is a disjunction of relative terms where one is more general in one respect and more specific in another, allowing all combinations of truth values. The author provides arguments for each ruling using conversion and absurdity. This classification is important for understanding how disjunctive propositions function in syllogisms and for identifying fallacies.

Key Terms
منفصلة
منفصلة حقيقية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the third type of disjunctive (e.g., 'stone or tree'), what is the ruling?
A) Both parts cannot be true together.
B) Both parts cannot be false together.
C) One part must be true and the other false.
D) Both parts can be true together.

Key Takeaway
The third type of disjunctive proposition forbids both disjuncts being true, corresponding to exclusive disjunction.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct a third-type disjunctive proposition about a real fiqh issue (e.g., 'This water is either pure or impure'). Verify that both parts cannot be true together.
?
Identify the third-type disjunction in the Qur'anic verse: 'Indeed, the religion with Allah is Islam' (3:19) – though not explicitly disjunctive, consider a scenario where a prophet says 'Either follow this or that' and analyze its truth conditions.

Fourth Type of Disjunctive: NAND Disjunction
Author enumerates five types of disjunctive propositions with examples and rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a fourth-type disjunctive proposition and verify its truth condition: both parts cannot be false together, but both can be true.

Core Idea
Fourth-type disjunction: both parts cannot be false together, but both can be true.

The fourth type of disjunctive proposition uses negated terms, e.g., 'either not stone or not tree'. Its ruling is that both parts cannot be false at the same time, but they can both be true. We explore the author's argument using conversion and absurdity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disjuncts with negation
The disjuncts are 'not stone' and 'not tree'. Note that they are negations of the original terms.
This type is essentially the logical NAND (not both) or equivalently, at least one is false.
2
Understand the ruling
The author says it is impossible for both disjuncts to be false together, but possible for both to be true together.
This means at least one of the negations is true, i.e., at least one of the original terms is false.
3
Follow the proof
The proof shows that if both were false, then the original terms would both be true (contradicting the exclusive nature?) Actually, if 'not stone' is false, then stone is true; similarly for tree. Then stone and tree would be true together, which is not allowed in the third type? But here the fourth type is separate. The author argues that if 'not stone' implies 'not tree', then 'not stone' would be equivalent to 'not tree', but they are not equivalent.
The reasoning ensures the ruling holds.
4
Apply to Islamic examples
Think of a fiqh ruling: 'Either this act is not wajib or it is not haram.' This allows both to be true (i.e., it could be mustahabb or mubah) but forbids both being false (i.e., it cannot be both wajib and haram simultaneously).

Source Text

Third example: This is either a stone or a tree, for the matter is that it is either a stone or not, and stones are from trees? Therefore the first: if one of the two parts is true, the other is true? because it is more specific than it. Fifth Type of Disjunctive: Relative Disjunction
Author enumerates five types of disjunctive propositions with examples and rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to recognize a fifth-type disjunctive proposition and describe its truth condition: all combinations of truth and falsity are possible.

Core Idea
Fifth-type disjunction: both parts can be true, both false, or one true and the other false, with no restrictions.

The fifth type of disjunctive proposition involves relative terms where one term is more general in one respect and more specific in another. This disjunction does not impose any restriction on truth values; all four combinations are possible.

Guided Path
1
Understand relative terms
The terms in a fifth-type disjunction are relative (idafi), meaning they have overlapping generalities. For example, 'father' and 'son' are relative: a man can be both a father and a son.
Relative terms are such that one can be more general in one aspect and more specific in another.
2
Identify the disjuncts
The disjuncts are two relative terms. The author says they allow conjunction and disjunction without prohibition.
This means no truth combination is excluded.
3
List the four truth combinations
Both true, both false, first true/second false, first false/second true are all possible.
This is unlike the third and fourth types which impose restrictions.
4
Apply to Islamic sciences
In Arabic grammar, terms like 'isim' (noun) and 'fi'l' (verb) are relative: a word can be both a noun and a verb? No, but in terms of 'agent' and 'patient', a person can be both. In fiqh, a person can be both a witness and a defendant.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The third type is a real disjunction where the two parts are mutually exclusive and exhaustive? Actually the text describes it as 'impossibility of both being true' but the example 'stone or tree' is not mutually exclusive? The author says 'it is either a stone or a tree' and claims that because stone implies tree? This is confusing. The fourth type is 'either not stone or not tree' where both parts can be true together (since something can be neither stone nor tree) but cannot both be false? The fifth type is a disjunction of relative terms where one is more general in one respect and more specific in another, allowing all combinations of truth values. The author provides arguments for each ruling using conversion and absurdity. This classification is important for understanding how disjunctive propositions function in syllogisms and for identifying fallacies.

The Three Types of Disjunctive Propositions
Author defines three types of disjunctive propositions with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of disjunctive propositions (المنفصلة الحقيقية, مانعة الجمع, مانعة الخلو) and determine their logical rulings from examples.

Core Idea
True exclusive disjunction allows only one truth; other types allow different combinations of truth and falsity.

In logic, disjunctive propositions are not all the same. Some exclude both parts being true, some exclude both false, and some do both. This lesson unpacks the three key types, their rulings, and a common confusion about the number of parts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types from definitions
Read the definitions: the true exclusive (الحقيقية) prevents both true together and false together; مانعة الجمع prevents only true together; مانعة الخلو prevents only false together. Note the difference in what they exclude.
2
Analyze the examples given
The third example 'this is either a stone or a tree' is a true exclusive: the two parts cannot be both true or both false. The fourth example uses negatives: 'this is either not a stone or not a tree' prevents both false (since if both false, it would be both stone and tree) but allows both true (if it is neither).
The fifth example is not a proper disjunction as it allows both truth and falsity.
3
Understand the ruling on the true exclusive's parts
The true exclusive has exactly two parts. If a disjunction appears to have more, it is actually a series of two-part disjunctions in potential, not a single multi-part disjunction.
This resolves the confusion: 'either stone, tree, or grass' is really two two-part disjunctions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحكمها للمنع من الجمع والحلو وهي المفصلة الحقيقية. مثال الثالث: هذا الشي إما أن يكون حجرا أو شجرا، وحكمها استحالة صدق الجزين فالمكان كنا. وأما الثاني فلانه لو كان كلما كذب المجر صدق الشير كان البحر ما ويا اللي وكان أخص منه هذا خلف. مثال الرابع: هذا الشي مالات لا يكون حجرا وإما أن لا يكون شحوا، وحكمه امتناع اجتماع من بها الكذب وإمكان اجتماع على الصدق. وأما الخامس فان الطرفين يفتح اجتماعها على الصدق والكذب معا. والمنفصلة الحقيقية لا تكون إلا ذات جزئين. لكن الجائز أن نقسم أحدهما أو كلاهما إلى قسمين، فإذا ركبت المنفصلة منها ظن أنها أجزاء أكثر من جزين، وليس الأمر كذلك لأن هناك منفصلات بالقوة مترتبة ليس لكل واحد منها إلا جزآن فقط.
And its ruling is prevention of conjunction and disjunction, and it is the true exclusive disjunction. The third example: this thing is either a stone or a tree, and its ruling is the impossibility of both parts being true simultaneously. As for the second, because if whenever the first part is false the second is true, then the second would be equivalent to the first in extension, and that is a contradiction. The fourth example: this thing is either not a stone or not a tree, and its ruling is the impossibility of both being false together and the possibility of both being true together. As for the fifth, its two parts allow both truth and falsity together. The true exclusive disjunction only has two parts. But it is possible to divide one or both of them into two parts, so when the disjunction is composed from them, one might think it has more than two parts, but it is not so because there are potential disjunctions arranged in order, each having only two parts.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage defines the three main types of disjunctive propositions and explains their logical rulings. The author begins by stating that the true exclusive disjunction (al-munfasila al-haqiqiyya) prevents both conjunction (i.e., both parts cannot be true together) and disjunction (i.e., both parts cannot be false together). Then he gives examples. The third example is a true exclusive: 'This thing is either a stone or a tree.' Here, it is impossible for both to be true, and it is also impossible for both to be false (since if it is not a stone, it must be a tree, and vice versa). The author then addresses a potential objection: if whenever the first part is false the second is true, then the second would be equivalent to the first in denotation, which is a contradiction because the first is more specific. The fourth example is a disjunction of negatives: 'This thing is either not a stone or not a tree.' This type prevents both from being false together (since if both were false, the thing would be both stone and tree, impossible) but allows both to be true together (e.g., if it is neither stone nor tree). The fifth example is a disjunction where both parts can be true together and both false together; this is not a proper disjunction but a distinction without mutual exclusivity. Finally, the author clarifies a common confusion: while a true exclusive disjunction seems to have only two parts, one might think it can have more if one part is subdivided (e.g., 'either stone or tree or grass'), but in reality such a compound is a series of potential disjunctions, each with only two parts.

منفصلة حقيقية
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو

Key Takeaway

Compound Conditionals: Composition and Number of Forms
Enumeration of compound conditional forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the possible compositions of compound conditionals and distinguish the number of forms for conjunctive vs disjunctive conditionals.

Core Idea
Compound conditionals can be built from categoricals and other conditionals in nine or six forms.

In this passage, al-Razi explains how conditionals can be composed from other propositions, not just simple categoricals. He systematically lists the possible types of components and calculates the total number of distinct forms for conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals. Understanding this classification is essential for analyzing logical arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence where nested conditionals appear.

Guided Path
1
Identify the component types
Al-Razi mentions six possible basic combinations of components: two categoricals, two conjunctives, two disjunctives, a categorical with a conjunctive, a categorical with a disjunctive, and a conjunctive with a disjunctive. Each component itself can be a categorical, conjunctive, or disjunctive proposition.
The components are the antecedent and consequent of the compound conditional.
2
Calculate forms for conjunctive conditionals
For conjunctive conditionals (muttaṣila), the last three combinations allow two orders each because the antecedent and consequent are distinguished. This gives 2×3=6 possibilities. Adding the three symmetric types (where both components are the same kind) yields 9 total forms.
Symmetric types: two categoricals, two conjunctives, two disjunctives each count as one form.
3
Calculate forms for disjunctive conditionals
Disjunctive conditionals (munfaṣila) are symmetric in order, so each of the six basic combinations yields exactly one form. Thus the total number of forms is 6.
No ordering distinction for disjunctives.
4
Construct an example from each category
Practice creating an Islamic example for each type of compound conditional. For instance, from two categoricals: 'If the prayer time has entered, then prayer is obligatory.' From a categorical and a conjunctive: 'If the witness is just, then if he swears, his testimony is accepted.'
Use Islamic legal or theological scenarios for all examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

As for the shell in which the proposition is composed from both its sides, it is either a necessary of the two contradictories both positive together or both negative together, or a necessary of the positive is positive and a necessary of the negative is negative, conversely. And since they were from the days of wildness, we therefore [illegible] them. It is either composed of two categoricals, or two conjunctives, or two disjunctives, or a categorical and a conjunctive, or a categorical and a disjunctive, or a conjunctive and a disjunctive. And you have known that the conjunctive distinguishes the antecedent from the consequent, so necessarily each of the last three can occur in practice in two ways. So the conjunctive can occur in nine ways, and the disjunctive occurs only in six ways. Let us mention the divisions of conjunctives first: from two categoricals: if the sun is shining, then daytime exists. Commentary
This passage discusses compound conditionals, i.e., conditionals whose components are themselves logical propositions. The author first defines a 'shell' (qishr) where a proposition is composed of two sides. He notes that the necessary consequent can be in various relations, but the OCR is unclear. He then states a general principle: in conjunctive conditionals (mutassila), the antecedent is not naturally prevented from the consequent but by convention. The composition can take six forms: two categoricals, two conjunctives, two disjunctives, a categorical with a conjunctive, a categorical with a disjunctive, or a conjunctive with a disjunctive. For conjunctives, each of the last three combinations can occur in two orders (e.g., categorical as antecedent and conjunctive as consequent, or vice versa), giving 2 × 3 = 6 possibilities, plus the three symmetrical ones (dual categorical, dual conjunctive, dual disjunctive) make 9 total forms for conjunctive conditionals. For disjunctive conditionals, the number is only six because the components are not ordered (disjunction is symmetric). The author then begins to list examples, starting with two categoricals: 'If the sun is shining, then daytime exists.' This is a classic example of a conditional. The passage breaks off with an unclear example about necessary consequence.

Classifying Conditional Propositions: Muttasila, Munfasila, and Particle Position
Author defines conditional and disjunctive propositions and discusses their structure and parts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between conditional (shartiyya muttasila) and disjunctive (shartiyya munfasila) propositions, and identify the position of the connective particle (before or after the subject) in each type.

Core Idea
Conditional and disjunctive propositions differ not only in meaning but also in the position of their connective particle, affecting their logical form.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi defines the two main types of conditional propositions: the conditional (muttasila) and the disjunctive (munfasila). He also explains that the connective particle (like 'if' or 'either') can appear either before or after the subject, creating four subtypes. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for later recognizing valid and invalid syllogisms involving conditionals, which are common in Islamic theological and juristic reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main types
Find the Arabic terms for conditional (شرطية متصلة) and disjunctive (شرطية منفصلة) in the passage. Note the examples given: e.g., 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists' (muttasila) and 'Either the world is eternal or the Creator exists' (munfasila).
2
Recognize the parts of each type
For muttasila, identify the muqaddam (antecedent) and tali (consequent). For munfasila, note that it has two or more disjuncts. The passage discusses how complexity arises when parts contain multiple propositions.
Important: a single conditional can have a complex antecedent or consequent without losing its unity.
3
Determine the position of the connective particle
The passage distinguishes four types based on whether the particle (حرف الاتصال or حرف الانفصال) occurs before or after the subject. For example, 'Every number is either even or odd' has the particle after the subject, while 'Either every number is even or every number is odd' has it before.
This classification affects whether the proposition is closer to a categorical (ḥamliyya) or remains a true conditional.
4
Understand the logical force of each position
When the particle follows the subject (e.g., 'Every number is either even or odd'), the author says it has 'the force of a categorical' (qūwat al-ḥamliyya). When it precedes, it is a genuine conditional/disjunctive. Recognize the difference in logical behavior.
This is crucial for later conversions and syllogistic reasoning.
5
Apply the distinction to new examples
Practice by taking propositions from Islamic sciences (e.g., fiqh conditions) and classifying them as muttasila or munfasila, noting particle position. This scaffolds the skill of parsing logical statements.
Example: 'If the prayer is valid, then the ablution was complete' is muttasila with particle before subject.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإنه حتى وجد وجد المعلول، فين كونه علة، وإن لا توجد المعلول منافاة. فتح أنه إما أن لا تكون طلوع الشمس النهار، وإما أن تكون كلما كانت الشمس طالعة كان النهار موجودا. كل من علية ومنفصلة؛ لأن كل طبيعة تلزمها بين قسمة، كان عدم تلك القسمة ووجود تلك الطبيعة معائلة لاستحالة وجود الملهم عند عدم اللازم. فقولنا: هذا إما أن يكون زوجا وإما أن يكون مركبا، وإما أن لا يكون عددا. من متصلة ومنفصلة. ومعناه أنك ستعرف أن المتصلة والمنفصلة كيف ينبغي أن تكون. ثم فتح تركيب المنفصلة عنها عقولك: إما أن يكون الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود، وإما أن تكون الشمس طالعة وإما أن يكون النهار موجودا. إلى أجزاء الشركة قد عرفت أن المنفصلة كيف تكون ذات جوان أو أكثر. وأما المتصلة فهي لا محالة ذات جوان مقدم. وقال: فإن كان كل أحد منها قضية واحدة، فلا سي في وحدة المتصلة. فإما أن يكون المنكر قضانا كنة، فإن كان في المقدم كانت المتصلة ويكون مجموعه أحقدما واحدا، وإن كان في التالي لكن قضية واحدة بل قضايا كثيرة، لأنه لا فرق بين أن تقول: إن وجد كذا وجد كذا، أو أن ترد لكل واحد منها شرطية واحدة. فقيل: قد يكون الثاني قضايا كثيرة والمتصلة واحدة، كقولنا: إن كان يوجد هذا مع عدم ذاك وذاك مع عدم هذا، فليس هذا بشرط ذاك ولا ذاك شرط هذا. وعند هذا بالحقيقة قضيتان مخالف كل واحدة من الأخرى المقدمها وتاليها. فقولك: إن كان يوجد هنا مع عدم ذاك، بالامه أن هذا غير مشروط بذلك، وهذه قضية قاعة. وإذا أذكرت الجانب الآخر كان قضية أخرى غير الأولى في كل واحد من جدي الشرطية. أما كون مشاركا للآخر جز به أو في احدها أو لا بشاركة رخ من جايه اصلا ماك الاول من المتصلة: إن كان كلا فبعضرات، ومن المنفصلة: إما أن يكون غراب وإما أن يكون كلاب. ومثال الثاني: أما الموضوع من المتصلة: إن كان كل إنسان وانا فكل إنسان جر، ومن المنفصلة: إما أن يكون هذا التى قدما للمحدثا، وأما المحول من المتصلة: إن كان كل إنسان حيوانا فكل ناطق حيوان، ومن المنفصلة: إما أن يكون التواجد في هذا المحلات أو البياض فيه. ومثال الثالث: من المتصلة: كلما كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود، ومن المنفصلة: إما أن يكون العالم قديما أو الصانع موجودا. المتصلة والمنفصلة قد يكون حرف الاتصال والانفصال فيها قبل الموضوع وقد يكون بعده، فهي أقسام أربعة: في المتصل الذي عرف الاتصال فيه بعد الموضوع، كقولك: الشمس كلما كانت طالعة كان النهار موجودا، وهو قريب من الحمل. وأما الذي حرف الاتصال فيه قبل الموضوع، كقولك: إن كانت الشمس طالعة فالنهار موجود، وظاهر أنه حملية وإن كانت الحملة لازمة لما. وهاتان القضيتان معاكسان. الذي حرف الانفصال فيه بعد الموضوع، كقولك: كل عدد ما إما أن يكون زوجا وإما أن يكون فردا. ومعناه أن كل واحد ما يقال له عدد لا تخلو من هذين الوصفين، وهو قوة الحملية. كانك قلت: العدد شيء من شأنه أن لا يخلو عن هذين الأمرين. الذي عرف الانفصال فيه قبل الموضوع، كقولك: إما أن يكون كل عدد زوجا وإما أن يكون كل عدد فردا. والفرق بين هذه وما قبلها لأن هذه منفصلة مانعة من الجمع، فقولك: إما أن يكون كل وإما أن تكون كل، لا يجتمع طرفاه على الصدق.
For it is such that if the effect exists, then its being a cause exists, and if the effect does not exist, there is a contradiction. Then you know that it is either that the rising of the sun is not the daytime, or that whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists. Each of these is a conditional and disjunctive; because every nature entails a division between them, the absence of that division and the existence of that nature are concomitant due to the impossibility of the concomitant's existence when the necessary condition is absent. So our statement: 'This is either even or composite, or it is not a number' is a conditional and disjunctive. Its meaning is that you will know how the conditional and disjunctive ought to be. Then you construct the disjunctive from them in your mind: either the sun is rising and daytime exists, or the sun is rising and daytime does not exist, into parts of the shared [elements]. You have known how the disjunctive has two or more sides. As for the conditional, it necessarily has a prior side. He said: If each of them is a single proposition, then there is no problem in the unity of the conditional. But if the denied is a proposition that is hidden, then if it is in the antecedent, the conditional is one and its aggregate is a single antecedent, and if it is in the consequent, it is not a single proposition but many propositions, because there is no difference between saying: 'If this exists, that exists' and between making each of them a separate conditional. It was said: Sometimes the second is many propositions and the conditional is one, as our statement: 'If this exists with the absence of that, and that exists with the absence of this, then this is not conditioned by that and that is not conditioned by this.' In reality, this is two propositions, each one differing from the other in its antecedent and consequent. For your statement: 'If this exists with the absence of that' implies that this is not conditioned by that, and this is a single proposition. When you mention the other side, it becomes another proposition different from the first in each of the two parts of the conditional. As for being a partner to the other partially or in one of them or not sharing at all, the first example from the conditional: 'If all are some, then some are not', and from the disjunctive: 'Either it is a raven or it is a dog'. The second example: as for the subject from the conditional: 'If every human is an animal, then every human is a walking?' and from the disjunctive: 'Either this is a foot for the originated?' The third example: from the conditional: 'If every human is an animal, then every rational is an animal', and from the disjunctive: 'Either existence is in this [place] or whiteness is in it'. And the fourth example: from the conditional: 'Whenever the sun is rising, daytime exists', and from the disjunctive: 'Either the world is eternal or the Creator exists'. Conditionals and disjunctives may have the connective particle of conjunction or disjunction before the subject or after it; thus they are four types. In the conditional where the connective occurs after the subject, such as your statement: 'The sun whenever it is rising, daytime exists', and it is close to the categorical. As for that where the connective is before the subject, such as your statement: 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists', and it is clear that it is categorical even if the categorical is necessary for it. These two propositions are opposite. As for that where the disjunctive particle is after the subject, such as your statement: 'Every number is either even or odd.' Its meaning is that every single thing called number is not free from these two attributes, and it has the force of a categorical, as if you said: 'Number is something whose nature is not free from these two matters.' As for that where the disjunctive particle is before the subject, such as your statement: 'Either every number is even or every number is odd.' The difference between this and the previous is that this is a disjunctive that prevents combination, for your statement: 'Either every is or every is not' – its two sides cannot both be true.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies conditional propositions (shartiyya). It begins by illustrating the relationship of causality and concomitance with an example: the effect's existence necessitates the cause's existence, and its absence entails a contradiction. The author then identifies two types of conditional propositions: muttasila (conditional, 'if...then') and munfasila (disjunctive, 'either...or'). He explains that every nature implies a division (qisma) between the two types. He provides examples: 'This is either even or composite, or it is not a number' is ambiguous between muttasila and munfasila. He clarifies the components: a muttasila has a muqaddam (antecedent) and tali (consequent), while a munfasila has two or more sides. The discussion then turns to the unity of the conditional when the antecedent or consequent is complex: if the antecedent is a single proposition, the conditional is one; if the consequent is multiple, it can still be a single conditional if the connection is maintained. He gives an example where the consequent contains two conditions, which in reality are two separate conditionals. He then categorizes examples based on the position of the connective particle (harf al-ittisal or al-infiṣāl) relative to the subject. He enumerates four types: (1) muttasila with the connective after the subject (e.g., 'The sun whenever it rises, daytime exists'), which is similar to a categorical (hamliyya); (2) muttasila with the connective before the subject (e.g., 'If the sun is rising, then daytime exists'), also effectively categorical; (3) munfasila with the disjunctive particle after the subject (e.g., 'Every number is either even or odd'), which has the force of a categorical; and (4) munfasila with the disjunctive particle before the subject (e.g., 'Either every number is even or every number is odd'), which is a truly disjunctive that prevents combination (mani'at al-jam'). The author stresses that in the last type, the two sides cannot both be true simultaneously. Throughout, he uses examples from logic and metaphysics (e.g., sun and daytime, numbers, world and Creator) but not from Islamic law specifically, though the principles apply. The passage is dense and requires careful parsing of the Arabic technical terms.

شرطية منفصلة
حرف الاتصال
حرف الانفصال
مقدم
تالي

C) Every number is either even or odd.

Key Takeaway
The key distinction between conditional and disjunctive propositions lies in their logical form and the position of the connective particle, which determines whether they function more like a categorical or a true conditional.

Definition and Truth Condition of Conditionals
Definition of conditional and truth condition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a conditional proposition and verify whether it satisfies the truth condition of necessitation (luzum).

Core Idea
A conditional is true not because its parts are true, but because the consequent necessarily follows from the antecedent.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi defines the conditional proposition (al-shartiyya al-muttasila) and clarifies its unique truth condition. Unlike categorical propositions, conditionals do not assert the truth of their components; rather, they assert a relation of necessitation (luzum) between the antecedent and consequent. Al-Razi provides examples across affirmative and negative scenarios to show that the truth of the conditional depends solely on this connection. Understanding this distinction is crucial for evaluating arguments that rely on conditional reasoning, such as those in kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Locate the Arabic term 'الشرطية المتصلة' (the conditional proposition) in the passage. This is the object of definition.
The passage also mentions the disjunctive (المنفصلة) but focuses on the conditional.
2
Extract the core relation
The author states that conditionals are based on 'اللزوم' (necessitation). Understand that luzum means the consequent must hold whenever the antecedent holds.
This relation is the differentia distinguishing conditionals from categoricals.
3
Analyse the examples
The passage gives three cases: both parts affirmative, antecedent affirmative consequent negative, and antecedent negative consequent affirmative. Check that in each case, the truth depends on the necessity of the connection, not on the actual truth of the parts.
For instance, 'Whenever this is human, it is animal' is true because humanity necessitates animality, not because every human actually exists.
4
Understand the truth condition
The truth condition is the existence of necessitation between antecedent and consequent. The contradictory of an affirmative conditional is that the consequent does NOT necessarily follow from the antecedent, not simply that the antecedent is true and consequent false.
This precision avoids confusion with categorical contradictories.
5
Compare with the disjunctive
The passage notes that the disjunctive (المنفصلة) is similar but based on 'المعاندة' (opposition) rather than necessitation. Recognise that this distinction is fundamental to compound propositions.
In a disjunctive, the truth condition is mutual exclusivity or exhaustiveness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلك الشَّرطياتِ بلزومِ قضيةٍ أخرى، سواء كان اللازمُ والملزومُ وجوديَّيْن كقولك: «كلما كان هذا إنساناً فهو حيوان»، أو كان الملزومُ وجوديَّاً واللازمُ عدميَّاً كقولك: «كلما كان هذا أسود فليس بأبيض»، أو بالعكس كقولك: «كلما لم يكن هذا خطَّاً فهو منحنٍ»، فاللزومُ في جميع هذه الأقسام حاصل. وإذا كان المرادُ مِن الإيجابِ في المتَّصلة هذا اللزومَ، كان سببُ هذا الاتصال عبارةً عن لزومِه [؟]، والفرقُ بين طلبِ اللزومِ وبين لزومِ الطلبِ [؟] ظاهر. فنقيضُ المتَّصلة الموجبة اللزومية هو أن تحكمَ بأنَّ ذلك التالي عيناً لازمٌ لذلك المقدم، إلا أن تحكمَ بأنَّ عدمَ ذلك المعنى لازمٌ لذلك المقدم فذلك موجبة. وكذا القولُ في المنفصلة: فإنَّ الإيجابَ فيها عبارةٌ عن الحكمِ بثبوتِ المعاندةِ بين الجانبين [؟].
The category of conditionals [is based] on the necessitation of one proposition by another, whether the necessary and the necessitator are both existential as in your saying: 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal,' or the necessitator is existential and the necessary is non-existential as in your saying: 'Whenever this is black, it is not white,' or the opposite as in your saying: 'Whenever this is not a line, it is curved.' So necessitation is present in all these divisions. And if what is meant by affirmation in the conditional is this necessitation, then the cause of this connection is its necessitation [?], and the difference between seeking necessitation and necessitation of seeking [?] is apparent. So the contradictory of the affirmative necessary conditional is that you judge that the consequent is necessarily necessitated by the antecedent, unless you judge that the non-existence of that meaning is necessitated by the antecedent; that is affirmative. And likewise the statement in the disjunctive: for affirmation therein is a statement of the existence of opposition between the two sides [?].
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage defines the conditional proposition (al-muttasila al-shartiyya) and specifies its truth condition. The author begins by stating that conditionals are based on the relation of necessitation (luzum) between two propositions: the antecedent (muqaddam) and the consequent (tali). He provides examples to illustrate the different possibilities: both antecedent and consequent can be affirmative (e.g., 'Whenever this is a human, it is an animal'), or the antecedent can be affirmative and the consequent negative (e.g., 'Whenever this is black, it is not white'), or the antecedent can be negative and the consequent affirmative (e.g., 'Whenever this is not a line, it is curved'). In all cases, the truth of the conditional depends on the existence of the necessitation relation, not on the truth or falsehood of the individual parts. The author then explains that the contradictory of an affirmative necessary conditional is not simply a denial of the connection, but a specific judgment that the consequent is not necessitated by the antecedent, or that the opposite of the consequent is necessitated. This precision is important because it distinguishes conditional logic from categorical logic. The passage also briefly mentions that the same principle applies to disjunctive conditionals (munfasila), where affirmation means judgment of opposition (mu'anada) between the two sides. This passage is an expansion (sharh) on the standard matn definition of conditionals, typical of al-Razi's work. Note: The OCR is severely corrupted, so some phrases are uncertain.

Key Takeaway

Truth Conditions of Conditional Propositions
Author explains when a conditional proposition is true or false.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the truth of a conditional proposition in Islamic logical argumentation by identifying the truth values of the antecedent and consequent.

Core Idea
A conditional is false only when the antecedent is true and the consequent is false.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi explains the truth conditions for conditional propositions (al-muttasila). Understanding these conditions is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic theology and jurisprudence, where conditionals like 'If it is wine, then it is forbidden' are common. We will walk through the four possible truth-value combinations and see which make the conditional true or false.

Guided Path
1
Identify antecedent and consequent
In a conditional 'If A then B', A is the antecedent (muqaddam) and B is the consequent (tali). For example, in 'If it is wine, then it is forbidden', 'it is wine' is the antecedent and 'it is forbidden' is the consequent.
2
List the four truth-value combinations
The antecedent and consequent can each be true (T) or false (F), giving four combinations: (T,T), (T,F), (F,T), (F,F).
3
Apply the rule from the passage
The conditional is true in three cases: both true, both false, or antecedent false and consequent true. It is false only when the antecedent is true and the consequent is false. Memorize: the only forbidden combination is T → F.
This corresponds to the material conditional in modern logic.
4
Distinguish accidental vs necessary conditionals
The passage notes that in accidental conditionals (ittifaqiyya), it is impossible for both antecedent and consequent to be false, while in necessary (luzumiyya) it is possible. For example, 'If water is heated, it expands' is necessary; 'If Zayd is in the house, then Amr is out' is accidental.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و أما المتصلة فالصدق قد يرد تحتها عن صادقين و عن كاذبين لأن مع كونهما صادقين يلزم صدقها و كذلك مع كونهما كاذبين لكون اللازم أعم من الملزوم و عن مقدم كاذب و تال صادق لاختلال كون اللازم أعم من الملزوم و أما كون المقدم صادقا و التال كاذبا فمحال لاستحالة أن يكون اللازم أعم من الملزوم و قد يكونان بحيث لا يتعين الصدق و الكذب فيهما كقولك إن كان عبد الله داخل الدار فزيد خارج الدار و الكاذب منها على وجهين: كون المقدم صادقا و التال كاذبا، و أما كون المقدم كاذبا و التال كاذبا فمحال في الاتفاقية و جائز في اللزومية.
As for the conditional, truth may occur under it from two true [propositions] and from two false [propositions], because with both being true, it is necessarily true, and likewise when both are false, because the necessary [is more general than that which is necessitated]; and when the antecedent is false and the consequent is true, because of the lack of generality of the necessary over the necessitated; but when the antecedent is true and the consequent is false, it is impossible because it is impossible for the necessary to be more general than the necessitated. They may also be such that truth and falsehood are not determined in them, like your saying: 'If Zayd is in the house, then 'Amr is out of the house.' The false [conditional] is of two types: (1) antecedent true and consequent false, and (2) antecedent false and consequent false is impossible in accidental [conditionals] but possible in necessary [ones].
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage defines the truth conditions for conditional propositions (al-muttasila). The author proceeds by enumerating the combinations of truth values of the antecedent (muqaddam) and consequent (tali) that make the whole conditional true or false. First, a conditional is true when both parts are true, both are false, or when the antecedent is false and the consequent is true. The reasoning given is that the necessary (the conditional as a whole) is more general than the necessitated (the individual propositions). Only when the antecedent is true and the consequent is false is the conditional impossible. The author then notes that in some conditionals, like 'If Zayd is in the house, then 'Amr is out of the house', the truth value is not fixed by the truth of the parts but by a constant relation. Finally, the false conditional is of two types: the standard case of true antecedent and false consequent, and the case of false antecedent and false consequent, which is impossible in accidental (ittifaqiyya) conditionals but possible in necessary (luzumiyya) ones. From an Islamic perspective, a conditional like 'If it is wine, then it is forbidden' is true when both parts are true (wine is forbidden) or both false (not wine and not forbidden), but false if it is wine and not forbidden.

Key Terms
المتصلة
المقدم
التالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) Antecedent false, consequent false

Key Takeaway
The truth condition for a conditional proposition is that it is false only when the antecedent is true and the consequent is false; in all other cases it is true.
