Truth Conditions of Disjunctive Propositions
Author distinguishes types of disjunctives and their truth conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of disjunctive propositions (haqiqiyya, mani'at al-jam', mani'at al-khuluw) and determine the truth condition for each.

Core Idea
Disjunctive propositions are not all exclusive; their truth depends on whether both, none, or exactly one part can be true.

In Islamic logic, disjunctive propositions (al-munfasila) are classified into three types based on the relationship between their parts. This lesson clarifies these types and their truth conditions, essential for evaluating arguments in fiqh and kalam that use 'either...or'.

Guided Path
1
Parse the three names
Identify the three Arabic terms: haqiqiyya (exclusive), mani'at al-jam' (preventing both true), and mani'at al-khuluw (preventing both false). Understand their literal meanings as clues to their truth conditions.
2
Extract truth condition for haqiqiyya
From the text, the exclusive disjunctive (haqiqiyya) is true when exactly one part is true. If both parts are true or both false, it is false.
This is the standard exclusive 'or'.
3
Extract truth condition for mani'at al-jam'
This disjunctive prevents both parts from being true simultaneously. It is true as long as both parts are not true together. It can be true even if both are false.
It is false only when both parts are true.
4
Extract truth condition for mani'at al-khuluw
This disjunctive prevents both parts from being false simultaneously. It is true as long as at least one part is true. It is false only when both parts are false.
This is the inclusive 'or'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و أما المنفصلة فالحقيقية و مانعة الخلو لا تكون كاذبة و الألزم كذب الطرفين و مانعة الجمع قد تكون كاذبة و أما أجناسها فالحقيقة تكون أحدها صادقا و البواقي كاذبة و مانعة الجمع تكون بين كذب الكل و لا نجد صدقها و مانعة الخلو بالعكس.
As for the disjunctive, the true exclusive [haqiqiyya] and the one that prevents both false [mani'at al-khuluw] are never false; the necessary [condition] is falsehood of both parts. The one that prevents both true [mani'at al-jam'] may be false. As for their genera, in the exclusive [haqiqiyya], one of them is true and the rest are false; in the one that prevents both true, there occurs falsehood of all, and we do not find it true; in the one that prevents both false, the opposite.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage classifies disjunctive propositions (al-munfasila) and gives their truth conditions. The author identifies three types: the exclusive disjunctive (haqiqiyya), which is true when exactly one part is true; the one that prevents both false (mani'at al-khuluw), which is true when at least one part is true; and the one that prevents both true (mani'at al-jam'), which is true when at most one part is true (i.e., not both true). The exclusive and the 'prevent both false' are never false because they cover all possibilities. The 'prevent both true' can be false if both parts are false. In Islamic legal reasoning, a disjunctive like 'This act is either obligatory or forbidden' (with both possibilities) is an exclusive disjunction: it is true only if exactly one is true. If both are false (the act is recommended), the disjunction is false.

Key Terms
المنفصلة
الحقيقية
مانعة الجمع
مانعة الخلو

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
Disjunctive propositions in Islamic logic are classified into three types, each with distinct truth conditions: exclusive (exactly one true), preventing both true (never both true), and preventing both false (never both false).

Determining Universality of Conditional Propositions
Author discusses how to determine universal and particular conditionals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a conditional proposition is universal or particular by examining its implication relation, not its terms.

Core Idea
In conditionals, universality is about the constancy of the 'if-then' link, not the scope of the parts.

The author explains that for conditional propositions, the key criterion for universality is the necessity of the implication (luzum) or negation (intifa'), not whether the antecedent or consequent contains universal terms. This distinction is crucial for correctly applying conditional statements in Islamic legal and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the antecedent and consequent
Separate the conditional into its 'if' part (antecedent) and 'then' part (consequent). For example, in 'If it is wine, it is forbidden', 'it is wine' is the antecedent and 'it is forbidden' is the consequent.
Ensure the conditional is clearly a conditional proposition, not a categorical one.
2
Determine the implication relation
Ask: Whenever the antecedent is true, is the consequent necessarily true? If yes, the implication holds in all cases. For 'If it is wine, it is forbidden', the implication holds for every instance of wine, so it is universal.
Ignore whether the terms themselves are universal or particular.
3
Check for indicators of universality or restriction
Look for words like 'every time', 'whenever', or 'always' that explicitly indicate universality. If absent, and the implication is constant, it is still universal. If the implication holds only at a specific time or under certain conditions, it is particular.
The author gives the example 'Whenever Zayd writes, Zayd moves' – this is false because Zayd may write without moving, showing the implication is not constant.
4
Classify the conditional
If the implication holds in all cases where the antecedent is true, the conditional is universal (kulliyya). If it holds only in some cases or at a particular time, it is particular (muhmala or shakhsiyya).
A particular conditional is also called 'important' (muhmala) or 'individual' (shakhsiyya) depending on the restriction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و إهمالها و شخصيتها ما أن الاعتبار في القضايا بالحكم لا بالمحكوم عليه و كذا الكلام في كليتها بالنية اللزوم و الانتفاء لا بقلية الطرف فإذا قلت كلما كان بعض الحيوان إنسانا فبعض الحيوان ناطق فالعصبية كلية لكون اللزوم كليا و إذا عرفت ذلك سهل عليك معرفة الإهمال و الحصر لأنه إن كان هناك ما يدل على كلية الإلزام أو الانتفاء فالقضية كلية و إلا فهي مهملة و أما الشخصية فهي التي يفرض الإلزام أو الانتفاء في وقت معين و للمتكلم الآن النظر في صور أربع من المتصلات الموهبة الكلية فإما أن تكون كلية لأن الموضوع في المقدم كلي فقلت كلما كان زيد يكتب فزيد متحرك فهذه القضية كاذبة مع أن موضوع مقدمها و تاليها شخصي و لا يلزم من كلية المقدم كلية القضية حتى يكون كلما كان المقدم أعم من التالي فالقضية كلية.
And its [the conditional's] particularity and universality are such that consideration in propositions is based on the judgment, not on the subject of judgment. Similarly, the discussion of its universality is based on the necessity of the implication and the negation, not on the universality of the part. So if you say: 'Whenever some animal is human, then some animal is rational,' the conditional is universal because the implication is universal. And when you understand that, it becomes easy for you to know particularity and restriction. Because if there is something indicating universality of the implication or negation, the proposition is universal; otherwise it is particular. As for the particular, it is that which requires the implication or negation at a particular time. And it is now necessary for the speaker to consider four forms of conditionals that appear universal. They may be universal because the subject in the antecedent is universal, so you say: 'Whenever Zayd writes, then Zayd moves' – this proposition is false even though the subject of its antecedent and consequent is individual. And the universality of the antecedent does not entail the universality of the conditional, so that whenever the antecedent is more general than the consequent, the conditional is universal.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage explains how to determine whether a conditional proposition is universal or particular. The key point is that universality is determined by the implication (luzum) or negation (intifa'), not by the individuals in the parts. The author gives an example: 'Whenever some animal is human, then some animal is rational' is universal because the implication holds in all cases where the antecedent is true, even though the parts are particular ('some animal'). Conversely, a statement like 'Whenever Zayd writes, then Zayd moves' is false because the implication is not constant (Zayd may write without moving), despite the subject being individual. Thus, a universal conditional must have a constant relation, not just universal terms. This is important in Islamic logic for formulating general rules, e.g., 'Whenever it is wine, it is forbidden' is universal because the implication (wine implies forbidden) holds in every instance, not because 'wine' is a universal term.

Key Terms
الكلية (al-kulliyya): Universality of a conditional proposition, meaning the implication holds for all cases where the antecedent is realized. [Note: Stable.]
المهملة (al-muhmala): A particular or unspecified conditional, where no indicator of universality of the implication exists. [Note: In categorical propositions, muhmala often means indefinite quantity; here it applies to conditionals.]
الشخصية (al-shakhsiyya): A particular conditional that holds only at a specific time or for a specific instance. [Note: Stable.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What determines universality in a conditional proposition according to the author?
A) The antecedent contains a universal term
B) The consequent contains a universal term
C) The implication holds in all relevant cases
D) Both parts are universal

Key Takeaway
The universality of a conditional proposition is based on the constancy of the implication, not on the universality of its component terms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Accidental and Necessary Conditionals
Author classifies conditionals into accidental and necessary and discusses truth conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between accidental and necessary conditionals and assess their truth conditions based on essence versus external existence.

Core Idea
The truth of a conditional depends on whether the connection is necessary or merely coincidental.

In this passage, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi divides conditional propositions into two kinds: accidental (ittifāqiyya) and necessary (luzūmiyya). He shows that an accidental conditional can be true by essence even if false externally, while a necessary conditional requires that the antecedent be taken in a way that its occurrence is possible. This distinction is crucial for evaluating arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
From the passage, locate the terms 'ittifāqiyya' and 'luzūmiyya'. These are the two main kinds of conditional propositions being defined.
The passage begins with an example related to God's knowledge, then explicitly states the division.
2
Understand truth conditions for accidental
The passage explains that an accidental conditional can be true in two ways: by considering the essence (al-haqīqa) or external existence (al-wujūd al-khārijī). The example 'Every time a man is rational, he is a man' is true in essence, but externally it may fail if rationality is absent.
Note the distinction between essence and existence: essence-based truth holds regardless of actual occurrence.
3
Analyze the necessary conditional condition
For a necessary conditional to be universally true, the antecedent must be taken in a way that its occurrence is possible. Otherwise, the universal statement would be false. The example 'Every time this is rational, it is human' requires that the antecedent be understood as 'rational in a possible way'.
If the antecedent is not qualified, the conditional may be false because some possible antecedents do not entail the consequent.
4
Examine the two problems with necessary conditionals
Al-Razi raises two issues: first, whether the nature of a single antecedent necessarily implies the consequent for all individuals; second, whether the entailment is by the essence of the antecedent or by something else, such as the nature of the differentia.
These problems highlight the complexity of necessary conditionals when dealing with universal terms.

As for the universal necessary conditional, it is only conceivable if the antecedent is taken in a way that its occurrence is not impossible. But if the [؟] is composed of two universals, that is not conceivable because when we say 'Every time every, then every' encompasses all subjects; how can that be true without the [؟] being true with it? Commentary
This passage defines two kinds of conditional propositions: accidental (ittifaqiyya) and necessary (luzumiyya). The author begins by analyzing the meaning of 'every time' (kullama) in a conditional, giving an example related to God's knowledge. He explains that an accidental conditional is true either by considering the essence or external existence. For instance, 'Every time a man is rational, he is a man' is true in essence, but if taken externally, it may fail because at some time a man may not be rational. The necessary conditional, on the other hand, is universal only when the antecedent is taken in a way that its occurrence is possible; otherwise the universal statement would be false. The author then raises two problems with necessary conditionals: first, whether the nature of a single antecedent necessarily implies the consequent universally; second, whether the entailment is by the essence of the antecedent or by something else. He discusses the entailment of a differentia (fasl) and the variation among individuals of the same nature. This passage is a typical logical analysis found in the works of Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, focusing on the nuances of conditional propositions.

True Disjunction: Mutually Exclusive and Exhaustive
Author explains true disjunction truth conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a disjunctive proposition is a true disjunction by checking its truth conditions.

Core Idea
A true disjunction requires exactly one alternative to hold — never both, never neither.

In logic, not all 'either...or' statements are equal. The author here defines the true disjunction (al-munfasila al-ḥaqīqiyya), which is the strongest type. It demands that the two alternatives cannot be simultaneously true or simultaneously false. This is crucial for constructing sound arguments in Islamic jurisprudence and theology.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two disjuncts
Find the two propositions linked by 'either...or' in the statement. For example, 'Either Zayd is in the house or he is not in the house.' The disjuncts are 'Zayd is in the house' and 'Zayd is not in the house'.
The disjuncts are typically simple propositions.
2
Check for mutual exclusivity
Determine if both disjuncts could be true at the same time. If they can, the disjunction is not a true disjunction. In the example, Zayd cannot be both in the house and not in the house, so they are mutually exclusive.
This condition is called 'māniʿat al-jamʿ' (preventing conjunction).
3
Check for exhaustiveness
Determine if both disjuncts could be false at the same time. If they can, the disjunction is not a true disjunction. In the example, Zayd must be either in the house or not; both cannot be false.
This condition is called 'māniʿat al-khuluw' (preventing emptiness).
4
Classify the disjunction
If both conditions hold (mutual exclusivity and exhaustiveness), it is a true disjunction (ḥaqīqiyya). If only one holds, it is a preventive disjunction (māniʿat al-jamʿ or māniʿat al-khuluw). If neither holds, it is a weak disjunction (māniʿat al-khuluw only or neither).
This classification helps in evaluating the validity of syllogisms using disjunctive premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The example given is problematic due to OCR, but the core idea is that a disjunction is true only when exactly one of the disjuncts holds. This is a standard treatment in Aristotelian logic as adopted by Islamic logicians.

Truth Conditions for Exclusive Disjunction
Author defines exclusive disjunction and its truth conditions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the truth conditions of an exclusive disjunctive proposition and distinguish it from inclusive disjunction using Islamic examples.

Core Idea
Exclusive disjunction forbids both truth and falsity together — a strict either-or.

In this passage, the author defines the exclusive disjunction (al-munfasila al-haqiqiyya) and its precise truth conditions. Unlike inclusive disjunction, which only requires that both parts cannot be false, exclusive disjunction also requires that they cannot both be true. This is the strongest disjunction, called 'man'at al-jam' wa al-khulu'. Understanding this distinction is essential for evaluating compound propositions in Islamic legal and theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key condition
Note that the author states the exclusive disjunction is true when the two sides are prevented from 'agreeing in truth and in falsity' — meaning they cannot be true together and cannot be false together.
2
Recognize the technical term
This type is called 'man'at al-jam' wa al-khulu' (preventer of combination and emptying). Memorize its Arabic name and its logical force.
3
Analyze the universal example
The author gives: 'A number is not both even and odd together.' This is a universal exclusive disjunction. Explain why it is always true: no number can be both even and odd, and every number must be either even or odd (in the context of integers).
This satisfies both conditions: not both true, not both false.
4
Analyze the particular example
The particular example: 'Sometimes man exists and sometimes he does not exist.' The truth condition is the 'third condition' where one side is true and the other false in the specific instance considered. Explain what 'third condition' refers to.
The disjunction is true only if exactly one part holds in the given instance.
5
Understand complete opposition
For a universal exclusive disjunction to be true, there must be complete opposition ('inad tamm') between the parts: the truth of one entails the falsity of the other, and vice versa. However, this opposition need not hold at all times; it is sufficient that it holds in some individuals (i.e., the proposition is about a particular instance).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و أما المنفصلة فإن الموجبة الحقيقية منها هي التي تصدق عند انتفاء اجتماع الطرفين على الصدق وعلى الكذب، فلا يكونان صادقين معا ولا كاذبين معا. فهي تمنع الجمع والخلو جميعا. و تسمى مانعة الجمع والخلو. و إذا كانت كلية كقولنا: ليس العدد زوجا وفردا معا، فهي صادقة. و أما المنفصلة الجزئية كقولنا: قد يكون الإنسان موجودا و قد يكون معدوما، فهي صادقة بالتقدير الثالث الذي لا اجتماع فيه و لا خلو، بل أحد الطرفين صادق و الآخر كاذب. و إذا صدقت الكلية فلا بد أن يتحقق العناد التام بين الأجزاء بأن يكون صدق أحد الطرفين مستلزما لكذب الآخر و بالعكس، و لا يلزم أن يكون ذلك دائما بل يكفي في بعض الأفراد. ثم إن أجزاء المنفصلة الحقيقية إذا كانت ثلاثة فإن الانفصال الحقيقي إنما يكون بين كل واحد و الباقين.
As for the disjunctive [proposition], the true affirmative one is that which is true when the two sides are prevented from agreeing in truth and in falsity, so that they are not both true together nor both false together. It prevents both combination and emptying [of both]. It is called 'preventer of combination and emptying'. If it is universal, like our statement: 'A number is not both even and odd together', then it is true. As for the particular disjunctive, like our statement: 'Sometimes man exists and sometimes he does not exist', it is true in the third condition where there is neither combination nor emptying, but rather one side is true and the other false. If the universal is true, then complete opposition between the parts must be realized, such that the truth of one side entails the falsity of the other and vice versa, but it is not necessary that this be always the case; rather it suffices in some individuals. Then if the parts of the true disjunctive are three, the real disjunction occurs between each one and the remainder.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage defines the truth conditions for exclusive disjunctive propositions (al-munfasila al-haqiqiyya). The author begins by stating that a true affirmative exclusive disjunction is true only when its two constituent propositions cannot be simultaneously true nor simultaneously false. This type is called 'man'at al-jam' wa al-khulu' (preventer of both combination and emptying). It is the strongest form of disjunction, requiring strict opposition. The author then illustrates with a universal example: 'A number is not both even and odd' — which is always true because no number can have both properties. For particular disjunctions, like 'Sometimes man exists and sometimes he does not exist', the truth condition is that one part is true and the other false in the instance under consideration. The author emphasizes that for a universal exclusive disjunction to be true, there must be complete opposition between the parts, but this opposition need not occur at all times; it is enough that it holds in some individuals (i.e., the proposition is about a specific case). Finally, the author notes that if the exclusive disjunction has three parts, the real disjunction is between each part and the remaining two, meaning that only one can be true at a time. This discussion is foundational for understanding compound propositions in logic and their application in argumentation, such as in fiqh rulings where a matter is either wajib, haram, or permissible.

Key Terms
المنفصلة الحقيقية
مانعة الجمع والخلو
العناد التام

Systematic Enumeration of Conditional and Disjunctive Propositions
Author enumerates possible forms of compound conditional and disjunctive propositions and calculates totals.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to systematically enumerate the possible forms of compound conditional and disjunctive propositions by applying the methods of classification by quantity, quality, modification, and order.

Core Idea
Enumeration in logic multiplies possibilities systematically to guarantee completeness.

In this passage, the author performs a combinatorial enumeration to count all possible forms of compound conditionals and disjunctives. Understanding this method is essential for recognizing the full range of propositional forms that appear in syllogisms and arguments in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Determine whether the proposition is a conditional (muttasila) or a disjunctive (munfasila). Each has its own enumeration rules, though they share common steps.
2
Classify each categorical component by quantity and quality
Each component (antecedent/consequent for conditionals, disjuncts for disjunctives) is a categorical proposition that can be singular, particular, or universal, and affirmative or negative. This yields six basic types (or, as the author states, eight when including a special case).
The author counts eight: singular affirmative/negative, particular affirmative/negative, universal affirmative/negative, and universal negative? The exact count is corrupted but the principle is clear.
3
Add the dimension of modification
Each component may be simple (mahmula) or modified (ma'dula), meaning its subject or predicate is negated. This doubles the number of possibilities for each component.
For example, 'non-man is writing' is a modified proposition.
4
Account for ordering and connection type
For conditionals, the antecedent and consequent are ordered, and the connection may be necessary (luzum) or coincidental (ittifaq). For disjunctives, determine whether the disjunction is partial (man' al-jam' only) or true (man' al-jam' wal-khuluw), and count the number of parts (usually two or three).
The author discusses tripartite disjunctives but focuses on binary ones.
5
Multiply all possibilities
Multiply the number of options for each component and for the relationship to get the total number of propositional forms. The author performs such multiplications to arrive at figures like 16 for the universal affirmative disjunctive.
The exact totals are obscured by OCR corruption, but the method is systematic multiplication.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed enumeration of the possible forms of conditional (muttasila) and disjunctive (munfasila) propositions, specifically focusing on those composed of two categorical propositions. The author begins by discussing a third supposition regarding disjunctives, where the parts are not necessarily contradictory. He illustrates with an example using variables J and D. He then considers the truth conditions when opposition is constant and when it is not. The discussion shifts to the composition of a disjunctive from two categorical propositions, noting that a true disjunctive requires complete opposition between the parts. When there are three parts, the opposition holds between one and the others. The author then addresses the enumeration of proportions (equality, excess, deficiency) and dismisses certain cases. The core of the passage is the systematic counting of all possible forms of the universal affirmative conditional. He breaks down the categorical components by quantity (singular, particular, universal) and quality (affirmative, negative), giving eight basic types. Then he considers whether each component is simple or modified (ma'dul), and the order of antecedent and consequent. Through multiplication of possibilities, he derives a total number, though the exact figure is obscured by OCR corruption. He also mentions that the same method applies to simple conditionals and to disjunctives, giving an example of the universal affirmative disjunctive with four main divisions based on the quality of the two parts, each with four sub-divisions based on quantity, totaling sixteen forms. The passage concludes by noting that this enumeration is used in other arts.

Enumeration of Universal Affirmative Conditional Types
Author calculates the 16 varieties of universal affirmative conditional proposition by combining antecedent/consequent qualities and quantities.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the 16 formal varieties of the universal affirmative conditional proposition by combining the quality (affirmative/negative) and quantity (universal/particular) of its antecedent and consequent.

Core Idea
A universal affirmative conditional has 16 forms because its two parts each have four categorical variants.

You already know that categorical propositions come in four types: A, E, I, O. Now, the universal affirmative conditional also has two parts—antecedent and consequent—each of which can be any of those four. By multiplying the possibilities, we get 16 distinct formal types. This classification is purely formal, not material: we ignore modal distinctions like necessity or existence. Let's walk through the enumeration step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two components of the conditional
A universal affirmative conditional (الموجبة الكلية المتصلة) has an antecedent (مقدم) and a consequent (تالي). The proposition asserts that whenever the antecedent holds, the consequent holds.
Example: 'Whenever the sun rises, it is daytime.'
2
Recall the four categorical types
Each component can be one of: universal affirmative (A), universal negative (E), particular affirmative (I), or particular negative (O). These are the four possibilities for any categorical proposition.
In Arabic: كلية موجبة, كلية سالبة, جزئية موجبة, جزئية سالبة.
3
Recognize that each component can be universal or particular
The antecedent and consequent can each be either universal or particular in quantity. This gives two possibilities per component, but combined with quality (affirmative/negative) we get four possibilities per component.
4
Multiply the possibilities
Since the antecedent has 4 possibilities and the consequent has 4, the total number of formal combinations is 4 × 4 = 16. This multiplicative combinatorics mirrors the logic of counting possible pairs.
The multiplication is justified by the independence of the antecedent and consequent in terms of quality and quantity.
5
Exclude modal considerations
Razi notes that modality (absolute, existential, necessary) is not considered here because it pertains to the matter (مادة) not the form (صورة). The 16 types are purely formal, based on composition (تركيب صوري).
Modality adds another dimension but is intentionally excluded in this enumeration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الموجبة المتصلة: إما أن يكون المقدم والتالي كليتين أو جزئيتين أو المقدم كليًا والتالي جزئيًا أو بالعكس. فلكل واحد من المقدم والتالي أربعة أقسام: كلية موجبة، كلية سالبة، جزئية موجبة، جزئية سالبة. فإذا ضربت الأربعة في الأربعة حصل ستة عشر. ولك أن تعتبر المقدم والتالي في الموجبة المتصلة بحسب كون كل منهما مطلقة أو وجودية أو ضرورية، لكن ذلك راجع إلى المواد، والمقصود هنا التركيب الصوري. فالحاصل أن الموجبة الكلية المتصلة تنقسم إلى ستة عشر قسماً باعتبار كيفية المقدم والتالي وكميتهما.
Then the universal affirmative conditional [proposition]: either the antecedent and consequent are both universal, both particular, or the antecedent is universal and the consequent particular, or vice versa. For each of the antecedent and consequent there are four divisions: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative. If you multiply four by four, you get sixteen. And you may consider the antecedent and consequent in the universal affirmative conditional as being absolute, existential, or necessary, but that pertains to the matter, while the intention here is the formal composition. So the result is that the universal affirmative conditional divides into sixteen parts based on the quality and quantity of the antecedent and consequent.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration (إحصاء) of the types of universal affirmative conditional propositions (الموجبة الكلية المتصلة) according to the quality (positive/negative) and quantity (universal/particular) of the antecedent (مقدم) and consequent (تالي). The author, Fakhr al-Din al-Razi, first establishes that the antecedent and consequent can each be universal or particular. For each, there are four combinations: universal affirmative, universal negative, particular affirmative, particular negative. Thus, the total number of forms is obtained by multiplying the four possibilities of the antecedent by the four of the consequent, yielding sixteen. The author then notes that one could also consider the modality (absolute, existential, necessary) of the antecedent and consequent, but that belongs to the material aspect, not the formal syllogistic structure. Hence, the focus is purely on the formal classification. The student should memorize the combinatorial principle: for a binary proposition (antecedent + consequent), the types are the product of the types of each part. This mirrors the earlier classification of categorical propositions into four types (quality × quantity). Here, the conditional adds another dimension, leading to 16.

Equivalence Between Disjunctive and Conditional Propositions
Author explains how a true disjunctive implies a conditional and vice versa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to convert a true disjunctive proposition into an equivalent conditional and vice versa, using Islamic logical examples.

Core Idea
A true disjunctive is implicitly a conditional; they are logically interconvertible.

In this passage, the author Fakhr al-Din al-Razi explains the fundamental equivalence between the true disjunctive proposition (al-munfasila al-haqiqiyya) and the conditional proposition (al-muttasila). Understanding this conversion is crucial for manipulating compound propositions in Islamic logic, especially in arguments that use conditionals or disjunctives as premises. We will walk through the logical mechanism and see examples from the tradition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types
Recognize that a true disjunctive (man' al-jam' wa al-khuluww) prohibits both conjunction and void, and a conditional expresses necessary connection (luzum) between antecedent and consequent.
The true disjunctive is exclusive; it is the strongest disjunction.
2
From disjunctive to conditional
If the disjunctive is true and one alternative is false, the other is necessarily true. This establishes a conditional: if not one, then the other.
This shows why every true disjunctive is a conditional.
3
From conditional to disjunctive
A necessary conditional 'if P then Q' means that it is not the case that P and not-Q simultaneously. This is equivalent to the disjunctive 'either not-P or Q' (or a negative disjunctive).
The conditional 'if P then Q' is equivalent to 'not-P or Q'.
4
Apply the father-grandfather example
The author's example: 'Either you are not a father or you are a grandfather' is a disjunctive equivalent to the conditional 'If he is a father, then he is not a grandfather'.
The disjunctive covers the same logical space as the conditional.
5
Test with an Islamic example
Convert the conditional 'If it is wine, then it is forbidden (haram)' into a disjunctive: 'Either it is not wine, or it is forbidden'.
This equivalence holds because wine and prohibition are linked necessarily.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاعْلَمْ أَنَّ كُلَّ مُنْفَصِلَةٍ حَقِيقِيَّةٍ مَوْجُودَةٍ فَهِيَ مُتَّصِلَةٌ مَوْجُودَةٌ، لِأَنَّ الْمُنْفَصِلَةَ الْحَقِيقِيَّةَ تَمْنَعُ الْجَمْعَ وَالْخُلُوَّ، فَإِذَا ارْتَفَعَ أَحَدُهُمَا ثَبَتَ الْآخَرُ ضَرُورَةً، فَيَنْعَقِدُ بَيْنَهُمَا اتِّصَالٌ لُزُومِيٌّ. وَكَذَا الْمُتَّصِلَةُ اللَّازِمَةُ تَرْجِعُ إِلَى مُنْفَصِلَةٍ سَالِبَةٍ، فَإِنَّهَا تَقْتَضِي أَنَّهُ لَيْسَ الْأَمْرُ كَذَا إِلَّا وَالْآخَرُ كَذَا. وَقَدْ مَثَّلُوا لِذَلِكَ بِقَوْلِهِمْ: إِمَّا أَنْ لَا تَكُونَ أَبًا وَإِمَّا أَنْ تَكُونَ جَدًّا، وَهِيَ مُنْفَصِلَةٌ فِي قُوَّةِ قَوْلِنَا: إِنْ كَانَ أَبًا فَلَيْسَ بِجَدٍّ. وَمَثَّلُوا بِقَوْلِهِمْ: إِمَّا أَنْ يَكُونَ أَبًا وَلَيْسَ جَدًّا، وَهِيَ مِنَ الْمُتَّصِلَاتِ فِي قُوَّةِ قَوْلِنَا: قَدْ يَكُونُ إِذَا كَانَ أَبًا فَلَيْسَ بِجَدٍّ.
And know that every true disjunctive (proposition) that is actual is a conditional that is actual, because the true disjunctive prevents conjunction and void; so if one of them is removed, the other is necessarily established, so a necessary connection occurs between them. Similarly, the necessary conditional reverts to a negative disjunctive, for it necessitates that it is not the case that this is so unless the other is so. And they gave as an example their statement: 'Either you are not a father or you are a grandfather.' This is a disjunctive in the force of our statement: 'If he is a father, then he is not a grandfather.' And they gave as an example their statement: 'Either he is a father or he is not a grandfather.' This is from the conditionals in the force of our statement: 'It may be that if he is a father, then he is not a grandfather.'
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage defines the logical equivalence between the true disjunctive (al-munfasila al-haqiqiyya) and the conditional (al-muttasila). The author begins by stating that every true disjunctive proposition (which prohibits both conjunction and void) is essentially a conditional proposition. The reason is that a true disjunctive requires that if one of the two alternatives is false, the other must be true — this establishes a necessary connection (ittisal luzumi) between them. Conversely, a necessary conditional (al-muttasila al-lazima) can be transformed into a negative disjunctive: for example, 'If it is A, then it is B' is equivalent to 'Either it is not A, or it is B.' The author then provides concrete examples using the relationship between being a father and being a grandfather. The first example: 'Either you are not a father, or you are a grandfather' is a disjunctive that is equivalent to the conditional 'If he is a father, then he is not a grandfather.' The second example: 'Either he is a father, or he is not a grandfather' is a conditional in the force of 'It may be that if he is a father, then he is not a grandfather.' These examples clarify how disjunctives and conditionals can be interchanged, which is crucial for understanding immediate inferences in logic.

منفصلة حقيقية
لزوم

Negation and Exclusivity in Categorical Propositions
Author discusses how negation affects exclusivity in categorical propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when negation in a categorical proposition removes exclusivity rather than repelling the predicate.

Core Idea
Negation sometimes lifts exclusivity, not the predicate.

In this passage, al-Razi explains that when negation enters a categorical proposition, its effect can vary depending on what is being negated. He distinguishes between 'رفع الحصر' (removal of exclusivity) and 'دفع المحمول' (repulsion of the predicate). Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly interpreting negative propositions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the proposition type
Determine if the proposition is categorical or conditional. For this lesson, focus on categorical propositions like 'كل الإنسان هو الضاحك'.
2
Locate the negation particle
Find where 'ليس' or 'لا' appears. In the example 'ليس الإنسان هو الماحل', the negation is on the whole copula.
3
Determine the intended effect
According to the author, if negation enters such a proposition, it primarily removes exclusivity (رفع الحصر) unless the intention is to repel the predicate, which then causes an excessive removal.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Test the rule with a fiqh-related categorical proposition: 'كل خمر حرام'. Negating it: 'ليس كل خمر حراما' removes exclusivity, implying some wine might not be forbidden. This shows the importance of correct interpretation.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The letter of negation may enter upon the antecedent of the connected, and the letters of exception upon its statement, and the letter of opposition, so it benefits the universality of the connected, like your saying: 'The sun is not rising except while daytime exists.' And likewise: 'The sun is not rising or daytime exists.' Remains the disjunctive: the letter of negation is brought in the antecedent and in the second condition it invalidates the multiplicity. Example: 'The non-existent is either spirit or individual.' And it is from the freedoms [؟]. And most of these discussions are not suitable for books of logic. And God is the flourishing, the second beginning for care [؟].
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage discusses the effects of negation (salb) and exception (istithna') on categorical and conditional propositions. It begins with an example of a categorical proposition: 'Every human is laughing.' The author notes that if the negation letter enters, it lifts the exclusivity (رفع الحصر) rather than repelling the predicate, as in 'Man is not the blamer.' If repulsion of the predicate is intended, the lifting of exclusivity happens excessively. Then he describes how the negation letter sometimes enters the subject, while the exception letter indicates the predicate, resulting in a proposition that implies unity and mutual implication, e.g., 'Man is only human and rational.' The passage then moves to conditional connected propositions (الشرطية المتصلة) introduced by 'lammā' (لمّا), which indicates the implication (لزوم) of the antecedent for the consequent. By excepting the exact antecedent, one can deduce the consequent, yielding two results: the implication itself and the exception of the antecedent. When negation enters here, it affects the antecedent, making it a negation of implication. The author then shows how negation and exception together can make a conditional universal, as in 'The sun is not rising except while daytime exists.' Finally, disjunctive propositions (الانفصال) are discussed, where negation is used in the antecedent and the second condition to eliminate multiplicity, illustrated by 'The non-existent is either spirit or individual.' The author concludes that such detailed discussions are not appropriate for logic books, perhaps indicating the limits of this field.

Key Terms
السلب (al-salb): Negation, a particle (حرف) that negates the predicate or the proposition.
الاستثناء (al-istithna'): Exception, a particle (حرف) that excludes some members, here used with negation to imply exclusivity.
الشرطية المتصلة (al-shartiyya al-muttasila): A conditional proposition that expresses implication (luzum) between antecedent and consequent.
الانفصال (al-infisal): Disjunctive proposition, where alternatives are presented, often using 'إما... وإما'.
اللزوم (al-luzum): Implication, the necessary relationship between antecedent and consequent in a conditional.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when negation enters a categorical proposition like 'ليس الإنسان هو الماحل', what is the primary effect?
A) It negates the predicate.
B) It removes exclusivity.
C) It affirms the predicate.
D) It creates a conditional.

Key Takeaway
In categorical propositions, negation can remove exclusivity rather than simply negating the predicate, a subtle distinction important for logical analysis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Write the negation of the categorical proposition 'كل مسكر حرام' (Every intoxicant is forbidden) using 'ليس'. Determine whether your negation removes exclusivity or repels the predicate, and explain based on al-Razi's distinction.
?
Find a Qur'anic example where negation is used with a categorical proposition (e.g., 'ليس كمثله شيء'). Explain how the negation functions in that verse.

Negation with Exception: Affirming Unity and Implication
Author explains how negation with exception creates a proposition of unity and mutual implication.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct a proposition using negation and exception to affirm unity and mutual implication of subject and predicate.

Core Idea
'Not...except' unifies subject and predicate.

Al-Razi introduces a special case where the negation letter enters the subject and the exception letter indicates the predicate, resulting in a proposition that affirms the unity of subject and predicate and their mutual implication. This is illustrated by 'ليس الإنسان إلا البشر و الناطق' (Man is only human and rational). This construction is powerful for expressing exclusive definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the negation and exception particles
Look for 'ليس' or 'لا' combined with 'إلا'. In 'ليس الإنسان إلا البشر و الناطق', 'ليس' is negation and 'إلا' is exception.
2
Determine the subject and predicate
The subject is 'الإنسان', the predicate is 'البشر و الناطق'. The structure is: predicate follows 'إلا'.
3
Analyze the meaning
Negation negates any other predicate, and exception affirms that the predicate fully captures the subject. This yields 'man is exclusively human and rational'.
4
Recognize the implied mutual implication
The author says it 'يفيد بوحد المحمول والموضوع وتلازمهما' (benefits the unity of predicate and subject and their mutual implication). So the subject logically implies the predicate and vice versa.
5
Apply to an Islamic definition
Use this pattern to define an Islamic term, e.g., 'ليس الصوم إلا الإمساك عن المفطرات' (Fasting is only abstaining from nullifiers). This establishes that 'fasting' and 'abstaining from nullifiers' are logically equivalent.

Source Text

Negation in Conditional and Disjunctive Propositions
Author discusses the effect of negation on conditional and disjunctive propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify how negation modifies the implication in conditional connected propositions and how it eliminates multiplicity in disjunctive propositions.

Core Idea
Negation flips implication and restricts disjunction.

Al-Razi extends the discussion to conditional propositions, where negation can affect the antecedent, and to disjunctive propositions, where negation eliminates one alternative. Understanding these moves is essential for evaluating arguments in kalam and fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Recognize conditional connected propositions
Conditional connected (الشرطية المتصلة) express implication. Often introduced by 'لما' (lammā), which indicates that the antecedent implies the consequent.
2
Understand negation's effect on the antecedent
When negation enters the antecedent of a conditional, it negates the implication itself, not just the antecedent. The passage says negation is 'على عدم لزوم الثاني من المقدم' – upon the non-implication of the consequent from the antecedent.
3
See how negation and exception universalize the conditional
Example: 'لا تكون الشمس طالعة إلا والنهار موجود' (The sun is not rising except while daytime exists). This creates a universal conditional: whenever the sun rises, daytime exists.
4
Apply to a kalam argument
Formulate a conditional about creation: 'لا يكون العالم محدثا إلا ويكون له محدث' (The world is not temporally originated except it has an originator). This implies a necessary relationship.
5
Handle disjunctive propositions
In disjunctives (الانفصال), bringing negation in the antecedent and in the second condition 'يصد التعد' (invalidates multiplicity). Example: 'العدم إما روح وإما فرد' – negation eliminates one option, reducing to a single alternative.

Source Text

Refuting Perception as Mere Presence
Refutation of perception as mere presence and exposition of knowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's argument that perception cannot be mere presence and identify the condition of immateriality required for perception.

Core Idea
Perception requires immateriality, not just the presence of a form.

In this passage from al-Razi's Mulakhkhas, he tackles a fundamental epistemological question: what is perception? The prevailing view among some philosophers was that perception is simply the presence of the form of the perceived object in the soul. Al-Razi forcefully argues that this cannot be, because inanimate objects also have forms present in them yet do not perceive. He introduces a crucial condition: immateriality. Let's walk through his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target view
Locate the position al-Razi is attacking: the idea that perception (idrak) is identical to the presence (husul) of the form of the perceived object in the soul.
This is the first part of the Arabic passage: 'لو كان الإدراك نفس حصول ماهية المدركة في النفس'
2
Find the counterexample
Al-Razi argues that if perception were mere presence, then anything that has a form present in it would be a perceiver. He points to inanimate substances (الأجسام الجمادية) that have forms but are not perceivers.
This is the key reductio: 'لما صدق على الأجسام الجمادية حصول له وكذب عليه إنه ملك له علما'
3
Extract the required condition
Al-Razi says that presence of a form is only perception when it occurs 'on a specific manner' (على وجه مخصوص), which he identifies as immateriality (التجرد عن المادة).
This is the positive thesis: perception requires the form to be abstracted from matter.
4
Follow the author's responses to objections
After stating his view, al-Razi addresses four objections (الأول, الثاني, الثالث, الرابع). For each, note how he defends his position, especially the third concerning immateriality and the fourth about union (الاتحاد) in intellection.
The responses are dense but crucial for understanding the full argument.
5
Connect to the nature of knowledge
The latter part of the passage turns to the definition of knowledge (العلم). Al-Razi argues that knowledge is a real, existent attribute (أمر وجودي), not a mere relation or absence, because we can distinguish it from other states and it cannot be non-existence.
This extends the discussion from perception to knowledge, showing both are real modalities of the soul.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكل الادراكات انه لو كان الأدراك نفس حصول ماهية المدركة في النفس [؟] لان الحس الموصوف بالسواد ملك له والثاني ظاهر الفساد فالمقدم مثله [؟] لان الادراك عند تحرير العبارة عن حصول الماهية المدركة للذات المدركة [؟] و ايجاد [؟] ترك و ايفا طاهية النفس مخالفة لماهية الحس [؟] فالاسلام من من كون خصو المعادن النفيس ادراكا لتولد ان تكون حصول الجبر اودا كاله و انت حصول السواد انما يكون ادراكا له اذا وقع ذلك الحصول على وجه مخصوص و هو التجرد عن المادة و لا كذلك الحصول في الحس [؟] و الف الصورة المعقولة اذا حلت في الذهن العاقل عقلت به و لا كذلك الشي الحالة الجاد [؟] لا نا نجيب عن الاول [؟] بان الادراك لو كان نفس الحصول لحان المسرك من له الحصوك [؟] لكن لما صدق على. العمادات المواد حاصل له و كذب عليه إنه ملك له علما ان الادراك ليس مو الحصول معز الثاني أن النفس محل الادراك و المحل مباين الحال فلو كانت حقيقي الادراك في الحصول فاينما تحقق الحصول كان ادراكا الا ان تجعل قول الوجود على الامرين بالاشتي [؟] ان انا إن جعلنا وجود الله نيرو هبته اشتغال ان مختلف وجود الماهية الواحدة محا خلاف المقابل وان جعلناه دابدا استحالات يكون بالاشتراك اللفظى بالاتفاق و الى امين التالفة وَعَن الثالث أن التجرد عن المادة إن كان هو الحقيقة الموجودة عادَ المحال اتى الحات ام المحاك لكن المختلف الي بالنسبة إلى المحل وان كان ناندا سلبًا كان او العاب حصل المطلوب و عن الرابع أن الاتحاد عمال على نامزات و هو خاص بالتعقل ان علمنا بذاتنا الصارف يكون من ذاتنا لو لا يكون و الاولا باطل اربعة الوحيد اما اولا فلانه يلزم أن كل من علم ذاتنا ان يعلم كونا عالمين بنواننا و الالى ظاهر الفساد و لاما ثاني فلانه يلزم أن ينعم علمنا بذاتنا و الثالي ظابط انسان و مو با طلك

For all perceptions: if perception were the mere presence of the form of the perceived in the soul... because the sense described with blackness possesses it and the second is clearly false, so the premise is similar... because perception, when the expression is refined, is the presence of the perceived essence for the perceiving self... and the presence of blackness is only perception of it when that presence occurs in a specific manner, which is immateriality, and not so presence in sense... and the intellectual form when it settles in the intellect, the intellect understands by it... thus we answer the first [objection]... if perception were the very presence, then the possessor of presence would be the perceiver, but since presence is true of inanimate substances and it is false that they have knowledge, perception is not presence. Second: the soul is the locus of perception and the locus is distinct from the condition, so if the reality of perception were in presence, then wherever presence occurs it would be perception, unless existence is said of them homonymously... Third: immateriality, if it is the existing reality, the impossible would follow... but if it is a negation, the required is obtained. Fourth: union is specific to intellection... as for our knowledge of ourselves, if it were a form distinct from ourselves, then if it is similar, likenesses would coexist, and if different, it would not be knowledge... then the discussion proceeds to the definition of knowledge: we know necessarily the sky and earth and our existence and the distinction between them and other things, and that depends on understanding the essence of knowledge, so the beginning is more worthy of being known... then this experiential state called knowledge cannot be a mere relation of existence, and the first is false for two reasons: we necessarily know its distinction from others, and if it were non-existence it would only be the absence of its opposite...
Source pages p. 211

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological discussion on the nature of perception and knowledge. The author begins by refuting the view that perception is identical to the presence of the form of the perceived object in the soul. He argues that if perception were mere presence, then any object that has a form present in it would be a perceiver, even inanimate objects, which is false. He then addresses several objections, including the relationship between the soul and its contents and the role of immateriality. The author further argues that our knowledge of ourselves cannot be a form distinct from ourselves, because that would lead to either a similarity or difference that undermines knowledge. Finally, he turns to the definition of knowledge (ilm), claiming that it is a real attribute (wujudiyya) rather than a mere relation or absence, because we distinguish it from other states and because non-existence cannot be knowledge. The discussion is dense and includes many technical terms such as al-husul (presence), al-tajarud an al-madda (immateriality), and al-ittihad (union). The OCR is highly corrupt, so the commentary is tentative.

Key Terms
الإدراك (al-idrak)
الحصول (al-husul)
التجرد عن المادة (al-tajarud an al-madda)
العلم (al-ilm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because inanimate objects would then be perceivers.
B) Because presence always requires materiality.
C) Because the soul is immaterial.
D) Because forms are eternal.

Key Takeaway
Perception is not the mere presence of a form but requires immateriality, and knowledge is a real attribute distinguished from non-existence.

Apply the same logical structure (modus tollens) to a different Islamic theological claim: 'If God were composed of parts, then He would be dependent. But God is not dependent. Therefore, God is not composed of parts.' Explain how this mirrors al-Razi's method.

Time, Increase, and Decrease: Mental Constructs vs. Reality
Critique of time's increase/decrease and priority

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct al-Razi's argument that time is not subject to increase and decrease and distinguish between mental constructs and external reality.

Core Idea
Time's increase and decrease are mental projections, not real properties.

Al-Razi continues his critique of the second purpose by attacking the claim that time can increase or decrease. He argues that what is judged to have increase/decrease is a mental hypothesis, not time itself. Then he delves into the nature of priority (qabliyya), questioning whether it is a real relation or a mental construct. This lesson unpacks that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent asserts that time is subject to increase and decrease. Locate this claim in the text.
The claim is implied in the purpose being refuted.
2
Find al-Razi's initial refutation
Al-Razi states that the claim is not self-evident (ḍarūrī) and that time is not eternal but perishable.
He argues that what is perishable cannot bear increase/decrease.
3
Extract the key distinction
Al-Razi distinguishes between what is subject to increase/decrease (a mental construct, farḍ dhihnī) and real time (al-zamān fī al-aʿyān).
The mental construct is not time itself.
4
Analyze the priority example
The example 'father is before son' is used to discuss whether priority (qabliyya) is a real relation or a mental one.
Al-Razi concludes that priority is a relation, not a property of time.
5
Evaluate the argument's strength
Consider whether al-Razi successfully shows that increase/decrease are merely mental and not real attributes of time.
The argument hinges on the distinction between mental and external existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وام الثاني فهو باطل لان من المعلوم ان القدر المذكور [؟] الا ان الزمان موجود اما لحركة فيكون [؟] كيف وجوه تغير حاصل عنده [؟] لانا نقول دعوى الضرورة باطلة لما بينا من الدلالة القاطعة على [؟] امر نابيت الحركة ثم ان تنانا عن هذا المقام ولكن مذهبكم ان المحكوم عليه بالزيادة والنقصان لابد وان يكون موجود او به [؟] من دلالة من استدك على ان للحوادث بداية بقبولها الزيارة والنقصان ثم من المعلوم ان الزمان ليس من الاحون القادة بل من الأحمر المنقضية واذا كان كذلك لم يصح العلم عليه بالزيادة والنقصان فاذن ما تريدون اثباته وهو الزمان مع الاحيان لا يمكن أن يكون محكوما عليه بالزيادة والنقصا وما كان محكوما عليه بك لك وهو الفرض الذهني لم يكن هو الزمان في الاعيات ثم لئن كلمت اله محكوم عليه بالزيادة والنقصان لكن لا الهزات يكون ذلك من الامود الفرضية الاعتبارية [؟] مثل في مسلة الحلار سان ذلك لكنه معارض بنفس الزمان فات بین ابتدار گردکان کاشانه امكانا يتح المثل ذلك المقدار ولا نع لما هو اعظم منه ولا ميلى ما هو اصغر منه و امكانا لقوافل منه لا نتع لذلك الزمان ولا لما هو اعظم منه وليالى بعضه واذا كان كذلك لزم ان تكون للرمان رمان فلس معلم ذلك من الامول الاعتبارية فقولو ا مثل الحركة بقالو اكون الاب قبل الابن معلوم بالضرورة ملك القبلية اخا إن كون نفس وجود الاب وتقدم الابن وسلو محال ان الوجود والعدم قبل لهما بعد و القبل ليس بعدا وراندا عليهما ف هواما ان يكون عدمًا محضا و هو محال لانه يفيض اللا قبلية التي هي عدم عض ونقيض العدم شوق او امرا تبونيا لخلع الذهن فقط لفرضا النجسة زوجا وهو باطل بالضرورة أو في الخارج، وهو اما ان يكون قائما بنفسه وهو مجالات القبلية من النسبة الإضافات الى العقل وجودها مستقلا اومو جود في محل ثم ان من المعلوم ان كل شي فانه لنقل هذه النسبة فان الاب مرحبا
And as for the second, it is false because it is known that the mentioned capacity [?] except that time exists either by motion [?] how [do you claim]? For we say: the claim of necessity is false because of what we have shown of decisive evidence on [?] the matter of negating motion. Then we [are done] with this topic, but your doctrine is that that which is judged to have increase and decrease must be existent or [?] from the evidence of one who argued that events have a beginning in accepting increase and decrease. Then it is known that time is not from the eternal things but from the things that elapse. If that is so, it is not correct to predicate increase and decrease of it. Therefore what you want to prove, namely time with its nows, cannot be subject to increase and decrease. And what is subject to that, namely the mental supposition, is not time in external reality. Then even if you say it is subject to increase and decrease, that is from the hypothetical posited matters [?] like in the matter of [??]; but that is contradicted by time itself. For [?] between [?] possibility of like that amount, and not for what is greater than it, nor for what is smaller than it, and possibility of [surpassing] it, not for that time nor for what is greater than it [?]. If that is so, then time would have time. And that is from the posited matters. So your statement like motion [?] the saying 'the father is before the son' is known by necessity regarding priority, because the very existence of the father and the precedence of the son [?] is impossible. For existence and non-existence do not have after and before, and priority is not an after and a before upon them. So it either is pure non-existence, which is impossible because it would necessitate the non-priority which is a non-existence and the opposite of non-existence is something, or it is a mental positing only for the supposition of [something] which is false by necessity, or in the external, and it either subsists by itself which is impossible, for priority is from relative relations to the mind independently or exists in a subject. Then it is known that every thing transfers this relation, for the father [?]
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage continues the critique. The author addresses the second possible purpose of the original argument: proving a state of time, such as that time is subject to increase and decrease. He argues that this too is false. The opponent claims that what is subject to increase and decrease must be existent (mawjūd), and they infer time's existence from that. But al-Razi counters by first denying that the opponent's claim is self-evident (ḍarūrī). He states that it is known that time is not eternal (qadīm) but perishable (munqaḍiya), so it cannot bear increase or decrease. What is judged to have increase/decrease is a mental construct, not external time. He then goes into a detailed discussion about priority (qabliyya), using the example of 'father before son' to analyze whether priority is a real relation or a mental one. He concludes that priority cannot be a pure non-existence nor a mental fiction, but rather a relation that exists in the mind or in external reality. However, the text is too corrupt to follow the full argument. This section seems to be an epistemological critique of how time and priority are conceived.

القبلية
الفرض الذهني

C) They apply only to eternal time, not perishable time.
D) They are impossible to discuss.

Key Takeaway
Al-Razi establishes that increase and decrease are mental constructs, not real attributes of time, preserving the philosophical distinction between mental and external existence.

Distinguishing Material and Efficient Causes
Razi critiques naturalistic causation by distinguishing material from efficient cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the material cause and the efficient cause in Razi's account of atmospheric phenomena and explain their relationship.

Core Idea
Razi uses material causes for explanation but insists the efficient cause is God.

In this passage, Razi provides a detailed physical description of thunder, lightning, and other phenomena using the concept of vapors. However, he ends with a crucial philosophical distinction: while the vapors are the material cause, the true efficient cause is God. This reflects a key principle in Islamic theology: natural causes are not autonomous but are subordinate to divine agency.

Guided Path
1
Identify the phenomena described
The passage covers thunder, lightning, shooting stars, and winds. Note how each is introduced.
2
Extract the material cause
Look for references to vapors (dukhan), their composition (sulphurous, oily), and their behavior (rising, igniting).
The material cause is what the phenomena are made of or how they occur physically.
3
Find the efficient cause statement
Locate where Razi says 'السبب الفاعلى فهو الله تعالى' (the efficient cause is God).
This is often at the end of the passage.
4
Compare the two causes
Note that material causes explain 'how', while efficient cause explains 'why ultimately'. Razi does not reject material causes but subordinates them.

Source pages p. 393

Commentary
This passage from Razi's Mulakhkhas is an extended physical explanation of several atmospheric phenomena—thunder, lightning, shooting stars, and winds—followed by a theological critique. The author opens by identifying thunder as the sound produced by lightning, which he describes as a fine smoke that ignites as it descends. He notes that lightning can be extremely hot and affect objects it strikes, sometimes cutting them cleanly due to its fineness. He then turns to the material causes, attributing these phenomena to vapors (adkhina) that rise from the earth, often containing sulphurous or oily matter. These vapors, when reaching cold layers of the atmosphere or the sphere of fire, ignite and produce lights (e.g., shooting stars) that may appear as dragons or tails. Razi also explains winds as the movement of vapors driven by temperature differences. Importantly, he critiques naturalistic explanations (referring to those who deny the sphere of fire) by arguing that while vapors serve as material causes, the efficient cause is God Almighty or spiritual forces. This reflects the interplay of natural philosophy and theology typical in Islamic thought: the physical description serves to demonstrate the patterns of causation, but Razi insists that ultimate agency belongs to God, guarding against purely materialist accounts. The passage is heavily corrupted in the OCR, making some details uncertain, but the overall structure remains clear: a descriptive account of phenomena, a material cause (vapors), and a final assertion of the divine efficient cause.

Checkpoint
According to Razi, which of the following correctly describes the relationship between vapors and thunder?
A) Vapors are the efficient cause of thunder.
B) Vapors are the material cause, and God is the efficient cause.
C) Vapors are the material and efficient cause.
D) Thunder has no material cause.

Key Takeaway
Razi affirms that natural phenomena have material causes but ultimately depend on God as the efficient cause.

Propositions
beginner
5 min
Explaining Thunder and Lightning through Vapor Ignition
Razi explains thunder and lightning as products of vapor ignition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct Razi's explanation of thunder and lightning using the concept of vapor ignition.

Core Idea
Thunder is the sound of lightning, which is fine ignited vapor.

Razi's account of thunder and lightning links them to the behavior of vapors rising from the earth. He describes lightning as a fine smoke that ignites and descends, producing thunder. This lesson unpacks the physical mechanism he proposes, showing how observational phenomena are traced back to material causes.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of thunder
Find the statement that thunder is the sound of lightning. Razi opens with 'صوت هو الرعد في الصاعق'.
2
Describe lightning's composition
Lightning is described as a fine smoke (دخان) that is extremely hot and can cut objects.
Razi compares it to a knife.
3
Trace the ignition process
The vapor rises, encounters cold air or the sphere of fire, and ignites. This produces the light and sound.
4
Note the effects of lightning
Razi mentions it can melt metal, leave black traces, and cut objects cleanly.
These observations support his description.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage from Razi's Mulakhkhas is an extended physical explanation of several atmospheric phenomena—thunder, lightning, shooting stars, and winds—followed by a theological critique. The author opens by identifying thunder as the sound produced by lightning, which he describes as a fine smoke that ignites as it descends. He notes that lightning can be extremely hot and affect objects it strikes, sometimes cutting them cleanly due to its fineness. He then turns to the material causes, attributing these phenomena to vapors (adkhina) that rise from the earth, often containing sulphurous or oily matter. These vapors, when reaching cold layers of the atmosphere or the sphere of fire, ignite and produce lights (e.g., shooting stars) that may appear as dragons or tails. Razi also explains winds as the movement of vapors driven by temperature differences. Importantly, he critiques naturalistic explanations (referring to those who deny the sphere of fire) by arguing that while vapors serve as material causes, the efficient cause is God Almighty or spiritual forces. This reflects the interplay of natural philosophy and theology typical in Islamic thought: the physical description serves to demonstrate the patterns of causation, but Razi insists that ultimate agency belongs to God, guarding against purely materialist accounts. The passage is heavily corrupted in the OCR, making some details uncertain, but the overall structure remains clear: a descriptive account of phenomena, a material cause (vapors), and a final assertion of the divine efficient cause.

What does Razi identify as the cause of thunder?
A) The collision of clouds.
